 100) "  Mar. 'Tis gone and will not answer."
 101) "  Ber. How now, Horatio? You tremble and look pale."
 102) "    Is not this something more than fantasy?"
 103) "    What think you on't?"
 104) "  Hor. Before my God, I might not this believe"
 105) "    Without the sensible and true avouch"
 106) "    Of mine own eyes.  "
 107) "  Mar. Is it not like the King?"
 108) "  Hor. As thou art to thyself."
 109) "    Such was the very armour he had on"
 110) "    When he th' ambitious Norway combated."
 111) "    So frown'd he once when, in an angry parle,"
 112) "    He smote the sledded Polacks on the ice."
 113) "    'Tis strange."
 114) "  Mar. Thus twice before, and jump at this dead hour,"
 115) "    With martial stalk hath he gone by our watch."
 116) "  Hor. In what particular thought to work I know not;"
 117) "    But, in the gross and scope of my opinion,"
 118) "    This bodes some strange eruption to our state."
 119) "  Mar. Good now, sit down, and tell me he that knows,"
 120) "    Why this same strict and most observant watch"
 121) "    So nightly toils the subject of the land,"
 122) "    And why such daily cast of brazen cannon"
 123) "    And foreign mart for implements of war;"
 124) "    Why such impress of shipwrights, whose sore task"
 125) "    Does not divide the Sunday from the week."
 126) "    What might be toward, that this sweaty haste  "
 127) "    Doth make the night joint-labourer with the day?"
 128) "    Who is't that can inform me?"
 129) "  Hor. That can I."
 130) "    At least, the whisper goes so. Our last king,"
 131) "    Whose image even but now appear'd to us,"
 132) "    Was, as you know, by Fortinbras of Norway,"
 133) "    Thereto prick'd on by a most emulate pride,"
 134) "    Dar'd to the combat; in which our valiant Hamlet"
 135) "    (For so this side of our known world esteem'd him)"
 136) "    Did slay this Fortinbras; who, by a seal'd compact,"
 137) "    Well ratified by law and heraldry,"
 138) "    Did forfeit, with his life, all those his lands"
 139) "    Which he stood seiz'd of, to the conqueror;"
 140) "    Against the which a moiety competent"
 141) "    Was gaged by our king; which had return'd"
 142) "    To the inheritance of Fortinbras,"
 143) "    Had he been vanquisher, as, by the same comart"
 144) "    And carriage of the article design'd,"
 145) "    His fell to Hamlet. Now, sir, young Fortinbras,"
 146) "    Of unimproved mettle hot and full,  "
 147) "    Hath in the skirts of Norway, here and there,"
 148) "    Shark'd up a list of lawless resolutes,"
 149) "    For food and diet, to some enterprise"
 150) "    That hath a stomach in't; which is no other,"
 151) "    As it doth well appear unto our state,"
 152) "    But to recover of us, by strong hand"
 153) "    And terms compulsatory, those foresaid lands"
 154) "    So by his father lost; and this, I take it,"
 155) "    Is the main motive of our preparations,"
 156) "    The source of this our watch, and the chief head"
 157) "    Of this post-haste and romage in the land."
 158) "  Ber. I think it be no other but e'en so."
 159) "    Well may it sort that this portentous figure"
 160) "    Comes armed through our watch, so like the King"
 161) "    That was and is the question of these wars."
 162) "  Hor. A mote it is to trouble the mind's eye."
 163) "    In the most high and palmy state of Rome,"
 164) "    A little ere the mightiest Julius fell,"
 165) "    The graves stood tenantless, and the sheeted dead"
 166) "    Did squeak and gibber in the Roman streets;  "
 167) "    As stars with trains of fire, and dews of blood,"
 168) "    Disasters in the sun; and the moist star"
 169) "    Upon whose influence Neptune's empire stands"
 170) "    Was sick almost to doomsday with eclipse."
 171) "    And even the like precurse of fierce events,"
 172) "    As harbingers preceding still the fates"
 173) "    And prologue to the omen coming on,"
 174) "    Have heaven and earth together demonstrated"
 175) "    Unto our climature and countrymen."
 176) "                      Enter Ghost again."
 177) "    But soft! behold! Lo, where it comes again!"
 178) "    I'll cross it, though it blast me.- Stay illusion!"
 179) "                                               Spreads his arms."
 180) "    If thou hast any sound, or use of voice,"
 181) "    Speak to me."
 182) "    If there be any good thing to be done,"
 183) "    That may to thee do ease, and, race to me,"
 184) "    Speak to me.  "
 185) "    If thou art privy to thy country's fate,"
 186) "    Which happily foreknowing may avoid,"
 187) "    O, speak!"
 188) "    Or if thou hast uphoarded in thy life"
 189) "    Extorted treasure in the womb of earth"
 190) "    (For which, they say, you spirits oft walk in death),"
 191) "                                                 The cock crows."
 192) "    Speak of it! Stay, and speak!- Stop it, Marcellus!"
 193) "  Mar. Shall I strike at it with my partisan?"
 194) "  Hor. Do, if it will not stand."
 195) "  Ber. 'Tis here!"
 196) "  Hor. 'Tis here!"
 197) "  Mar. 'Tis gone!"
 198) "                                                     Exit Ghost."
 199) "    We do it wrong, being so majestical,"
 200) "    To offer it the show of violence;"
 201) "    For it is as the air, invulnerable,"
 202) "    And our vain blows malicious mockery."
 203) "  Ber. It was about to speak, when the cock crew."
 204) "  Hor. And then it started, like a guilty thing  "
 205) "    Upon a fearful summons. I have heard"
 206) "    The cock, that is the trumpet to the morn,"
 207) "    Doth with his lofty and shrill-sounding throat"
 208) "    Awake the god of day; and at his warning,"
 209) "    Whether in sea or fire, in earth or air,"
 210) "    Th' extravagant and erring spirit hies"
 211) "    To his confine; and of the truth herein"
 212) "    This present object made probation."
 213) "  Mar. It faded on the crowing of the cock."
 214) "    Some say that ever, 'gainst that season comes"
 215) "    Wherein our Saviour's birth is celebrated,"
 216) "    The bird of dawning singeth all night long;"
 217) "    And then, they say, no spirit dare stir abroad,"
 218) "    The nights are wholesome, then no planets strike,"
 219) "    No fairy takes, nor witch hath power to charm,"
 220) "    So hallow'd and so gracious is the time."
 221) "  Hor. So have I heard and do in part believe it."
 222) "    But look, the morn, in russet mantle clad,"
 223) "    Walks o'er the dew of yon high eastward hill."
 224) "    Break we our watch up; and by my advice  "
 225) "    Let us impart what we have seen to-night"
 226) "    Unto young Hamlet; for, upon my life,"
 227) "    This spirit, dumb to us, will speak to him."
 228) "    Do you consent we shall acquaint him with it,"
 229) "    As needful in our loves, fitting our duty?"
 230) "    Let's do't, I pray; and I this morning know"
 231) "    Where we shall find him most conveniently.           Exeunt."
 232) "Scene II."
 233) "Elsinore. A room of state in the Castle."
 234) "Flourish. [Enter Claudius, King of Denmark, Gertrude the Queen, Hamlet,"
 235) "Polonius, Laertes and his sister Ophelia, [Voltemand, Cornelius,]"
 236) "Lords Attendant."
 237) "  King. Though yet of Hamlet our dear brother's death"
 238) "    The memory be green, and that it us befitted"
 239) "    To bear our hearts in grief, and our whole kingdom"
 240) "    To be contracted in one brow of woe,"
 241) "    Yet so far hath discretion fought with nature"
 242) "    That we with wisest sorrow think on him"
 243) "    Together with remembrance of ourselves."
 244) "    Therefore our sometime sister, now our queen,"
 245) "    Th' imperial jointress to this warlike state,"
 246) "    Have we, as 'twere with a defeated joy,"
 247) "    With an auspicious, and a dropping eye,"
 248) "    With mirth in funeral, and with dirge in marriage,"
 249) "    In equal scale weighing delight and dole,"
 250) "    Taken to wife; nor have we herein barr'd"
 251) "    Your better wisdoms, which have freely gone  "
 252) "    With this affair along. For all, our thanks."
 253) "    Now follows, that you know, young Fortinbras,"
 254) "    Holding a weak supposal of our worth,"
 255) "    Or thinking by our late dear brother's death"
 256) "    Our state to be disjoint and out of frame,"
 257) "    Colleagued with this dream of his advantage,"
 258) "    He hath not fail'd to pester us with message"
 259) "    Importing the surrender of those lands"
 260) "    Lost by his father, with all bands of law,"
 261) "    To our most valiant brother. So much for him."
 262) "    Now for ourself and for this time of meeting."
 263) "    Thus much the business is: we have here writ"
 264) "    To Norway, uncle of young Fortinbras,"
 265) "    Who, impotent and bedrid, scarcely hears"
 266) "    Of this his nephew's purpose, to suppress"
 267) "    His further gait herein, in that the levies,"
 268) "    The lists, and full proportions are all made"
 269) "    Out of his subject; and we here dispatch"
 270) "    You, good Cornelius, and you, Voltemand,"
 271) "    For bearers of this greeting to old Norway,  "
 272) "    Giving to you no further personal power"
 273) "    To business with the King, more than the scope"
 274) "    Of these dilated articles allow.            [Gives a paper.]"
 275) "    Farewell, and let your haste commend your duty."
 276) "  Cor., Volt. In that, and all things, will we show our duty."
 277) "  King. We doubt it nothing. Heartily farewell."
 278) "                                 Exeunt Voltemand and Cornelius."
 279) "    And now, Laertes, what's the news with you?"
 280) "    You told us of some suit. What is't, Laertes?"
 281) "    You cannot speak of reason to the Dane"
 282) "    And lose your voice. What wouldst thou beg, Laertes,"
 283) "    That shall not be my offer, not thy asking?"
 284) "    The head is not more native to the heart,"
 285) "    The hand more instrumental to the mouth,"
 286) "    Than is the throne of Denmark to thy father."
 287) "    What wouldst thou have, Laertes?"
 288) "  Laer. My dread lord,"
 289) "    Your leave and favour to return to France;"
 290) "    From whence though willingly I came to Denmark"
 291) "    To show my duty in your coronation,  "
 292) "    Yet now I must confess, that duty done,"
 293) "    My thoughts and wishes bend again toward France"
 294) "    And bow them to your gracious leave and pardon."
 295) "  King. Have you your father's leave? What says Polonius?"
 296) "  Pol. He hath, my lord, wrung from me my slow leave"
 297) "    By laboursome petition, and at last"
 298) "    Upon his will I seal'd my hard consent."
 299) "    I do beseech you give him leave to go."
 300) "  King. Take thy fair hour, Laertes. Time be thine,"
 301) "    And thy best graces spend it at thy will!"
 302) "    But now, my cousin Hamlet, and my son-"
 303) "  Ham. [aside] A little more than kin, and less than kind!"
 304) "  King. How is it that the clouds still hang on you?"
 305) "  Ham. Not so, my lord. I am too much i' th' sun."
 306) "  Queen. Good Hamlet, cast thy nighted colour off,"
 307) "    And let thine eye look like a friend on Denmark."
 308) "    Do not for ever with thy vailed lids"
 309) "    Seek for thy noble father in the dust."
 310) "    Thou know'st 'tis common. All that lives must die,"
 311) "    Passing through nature to eternity.  "
 312) "  Ham. Ay, madam, it is common."
 313) "  Queen. If it be,"
 314) "    Why seems it so particular with thee?"
 315) "  Ham. Seems, madam, Nay, it is. I know not 'seems.'"
 316) "    'Tis not alone my inky cloak, good mother,"
 317) "    Nor customary suits of solemn black,"
 318) "    Nor windy suspiration of forc'd breath,"
 319) "    No, nor the fruitful river in the eye,"
 320) "    Nor the dejected havior of the visage,"
 321) "    Together with all forms, moods, shapes of grief,"
 322) "    'That can denote me truly. These indeed seem,"
 323) "    For they are actions that a man might play;"
 324) "    But I have that within which passeth show-"
 325) "    These but the trappings and the suits of woe."
 326) "  King. 'Tis sweet and commendable in your nature, Hamlet,"
 327) "    To give these mourning duties to your father;"
 328) "    But you must know, your father lost a father;"
 329) "    That father lost, lost his, and the survivor bound"
 330) "    In filial obligation for some term"
 331) "    To do obsequious sorrow. But to persever  "
 332) "    In obstinate condolement is a course"
 333) "    Of impious stubbornness. 'Tis unmanly grief;"
 334) "    It shows a will most incorrect to heaven,"
 335) "    A heart unfortified, a mind impatient,"
 336) "    An understanding simple and unschool'd;"
 337) "    For what we know must be, and is as common"
 338) "    As any the most vulgar thing to sense,"
 339) "    Why should we in our peevish opposition"
 340) "    Take it to heart? Fie! 'tis a fault to heaven,"
 341) "    A fault against the dead, a fault to nature,"
 342) "    To reason most absurd, whose common theme"
 343) "    Is death of fathers, and who still hath cried,"
 344) "    From the first corse till he that died to-day,"
 345) "    'This must be so.' We pray you throw to earth"
 346) "    This unprevailing woe, and think of us"
 347) "    As of a father; for let the world take note"
 348) "    You are the most immediate to our throne,"
 349) "    And with no less nobility of love"
 350) "    Than that which dearest father bears his son"
 351) "    Do I impart toward you. For your intent  "
 352) "    In going back to school in Wittenberg,"
 353) "    It is most retrograde to our desire;"
 354) "    And we beseech you, bend you to remain"
 355) "    Here in the cheer and comfort of our eye,"
 356) "    Our chiefest courtier, cousin, and our son."
 357) "  Queen. Let not thy mother lose her prayers, Hamlet."
 358) "    I pray thee stay with us, go not to Wittenberg."
 359) "  Ham. I shall in all my best obey you, madam."
 360) "  King. Why, 'tis a loving and a fair reply."
 361) "    Be as ourself in Denmark. Madam, come."
 362) "    This gentle and unforc'd accord of Hamlet"
 363) "    Sits smiling to my heart; in grace whereof,"
 364) "    No jocund health that Denmark drinks to-day"
 365) "    But the great cannon to the clouds shall tell,"
 366) "    And the King's rouse the heaven shall bruit again,"
 367) "    Respeaking earthly thunder. Come away."
 368) "                                Flourish. Exeunt all but Hamlet."
 369) "  Ham. O that this too too solid flesh would melt,"
 370) "    Thaw, and resolve itself into a dew!"
 371) "    Or that the Everlasting had not fix'd  "
 372) "    His canon 'gainst self-slaughter! O God! God!"
 373) "    How weary, stale, flat, and unprofitable"
 374) "    Seem to me all the uses of this world!"
 375) "    Fie on't! ah, fie! 'Tis an unweeded garden"
 376) "    That grows to seed; things rank and gross in nature"
 377) "    Possess it merely. That it should come to this!"
 378) "    But two months dead! Nay, not so much, not two."
 379) "    So excellent a king, that was to this"
 380) "    Hyperion to a satyr; so loving to my mother"
 381) "    That he might not beteem the winds of heaven"
 382) "    Visit her face too roughly. Heaven and earth!"
 383) "    Must I remember? Why, she would hang on him"
 384) "    As if increase of appetite had grown"
 385) "    By what it fed on; and yet, within a month-"
 386) "    Let me not think on't! Frailty, thy name is woman!-"
 387) "    A little month, or ere those shoes were old"
 388) "    With which she followed my poor father's body"
 389) "    Like Niobe, all tears- why she, even she"
 390) "    (O God! a beast that wants discourse of reason"
 391) "    Would have mourn'd longer) married with my uncle;  "
 392) "    My father's brother, but no more like my father"
 393) "    Than I to Hercules. Within a month,"
 394) "    Ere yet the salt of most unrighteous tears"
 395) "    Had left the flushing in her galled eyes,"
 396) "    She married. O, most wicked speed, to post"
 397) "    With such dexterity to incestuous sheets!"
 398) "    It is not, nor it cannot come to good."
 399) "    But break my heart, for I must hold my tongue!"
 400) "          Enter Horatio, Marcellus, and Bernardo."
 401) "  Hor. Hail to your lordship!"
 402) "  Ham. I am glad to see you well."
 403) "    Horatio!- or I do forget myself."
 404) "  Hor. The same, my lord, and your poor servant ever."
 405) "  Ham. Sir, my good friend- I'll change that name with you."
 406) "    And what make you from Wittenberg, Horatio?"
 407) "    Marcellus?"
 408) "  Mar. My good lord!"
 409) "  Ham. I am very glad to see you.- [To Bernardo] Good even, sir.-  "
 410) "    But what, in faith, make you from Wittenberg?"
 411) "  Hor. A truant disposition, good my lord."
 412) "  Ham. I would not hear your enemy say so,"
 413) "    Nor shall you do my ear that violence"
 414) "    To make it truster of your own report"
 415) "    Against yourself. I know you are no truant."
 416) "    But what is your affair in Elsinore?"
 417) "    We'll teach you to drink deep ere you depart."
 418) "  Hor. My lord, I came to see your father's funeral."
 419) "  Ham. I prithee do not mock me, fellow student."
 420) "    I think it was to see my mother's wedding."
 421) "  Hor. Indeed, my lord, it followed hard upon."
 422) "  Ham. Thrift, thrift, Horatio! The funeral bak'd meats"
 423) "    Did coldly furnish forth the marriage tables."
 424) "    Would I had met my dearest foe in heaven"
 425) "    Or ever I had seen that day, Horatio!"
 426) "    My father- methinks I see my father."
 427) "  Hor. O, where, my lord?"
 428) "  Ham. In my mind's eye, Horatio."
 429) "  Hor. I saw him once. He was a goodly king.  "
 430) "  Ham. He was a man, take him for all in all."
 431) "    I shall not look upon his like again."
 432) "  Hor. My lord, I think I saw him yesternight."
 433) "  Ham. Saw? who?"
 434) "  Hor. My lord, the King your father."
 435) "  Ham. The King my father?"
 436) "  Hor. Season your admiration for a while"
 437) "    With an attent ear, till I may deliver"
 438) "    Upon the witness of these gentlemen,"
 439) "    This marvel to you."
 440) "  Ham. For God's love let me hear!"
 441) "  Hor. Two nights together had these gentlemen"
 442) "    (Marcellus and Bernardo) on their watch"
 443) "    In the dead vast and middle of the night"
 444) "    Been thus encount'red. A figure like your father,"
 445) "    Armed at point exactly, cap-a-pe,"
 446) "    Appears before them and with solemn march"
 447) "    Goes slow and stately by them. Thrice he walk'd"
 448) "    By their oppress'd and fear-surprised eyes,"
 449) "    Within his truncheon's length; whilst they distill'd  "
 450) "    Almost to jelly with the act of fear,"
 451) "    Stand dumb and speak not to him. This to me"
 452) "    In dreadful secrecy impart they did,"
 453) "    And I with them the third night kept the watch;"
 454) "    Where, as they had deliver'd, both in time,"
 455) "    Form of the thing, each word made true and good,"
 456) "    The apparition comes. I knew your father."
 457) "    These hands are not more like."
 458) "  Ham. But where was this?"
 459) "  Mar. My lord, upon the platform where we watch'd."
 460) "  Ham. Did you not speak to it?"
 461) "  Hor. My lord, I did;"
 462) "    But answer made it none. Yet once methought"
 463) "    It lifted up it head and did address"
 464) "    Itself to motion, like as it would speak;"
 465) "    But even then the morning cock crew loud,"
 466) "    And at the sound it shrunk in haste away"
 467) "    And vanish'd from our sight."
 468) "  Ham. 'Tis very strange."
 469) "  Hor. As I do live, my honour'd lord, 'tis true;  "
 470) "    And we did think it writ down in our duty"
 471) "    To let you know of it."
 472) "  Ham. Indeed, indeed, sirs. But this troubles me."
 473) "    Hold you the watch to-night?"
 474) "  Both [Mar. and Ber.] We do, my lord."
 475) "  Ham. Arm'd, say you?"
 476) "  Both. Arm'd, my lord."
 477) "  Ham. From top to toe?"
 478) "  Both. My lord, from head to foot."
 479) "  Ham. Then saw you not his face?"
 480) "  Hor. O, yes, my lord! He wore his beaver up."
 481) "  Ham. What, look'd he frowningly."
 482) "  Hor. A countenance more in sorrow than in anger."
 483) "  Ham. Pale or red?"
 484) "  Hor. Nay, very pale."
 485) "  Ham. And fix'd his eyes upon you?"
 486) "  Hor. Most constantly."
 487) "  Ham. I would I had been there."
 488) "  Hor. It would have much amaz'd you."
 489) "  Ham. Very like, very like. Stay'd it long?  "
 490) "  Hor. While one with moderate haste might tell a hundred."
 491) "  Both. Longer, longer."
 492) "  Hor. Not when I saw't."
 493) "  Ham. His beard was grizzled- no?"
 494) "  Hor. It was, as I have seen it in his life,"
 495) "    A sable silver'd."
 496) "  Ham. I will watch to-night."
 497) "    Perchance 'twill walk again."
 498) "  Hor. I warr'nt it will."
 499) "  Ham. If it assume my noble father's person,"
 500) "    I'll speak to it, though hell itself should gape"
 501) "    And bid me hold my peace. I pray you all,"
 502) "    If you have hitherto conceal'd this sight,"
 503) "    Let it be tenable in your silence still;"
 504) "    And whatsoever else shall hap to-night,"
 505) "    Give it an understanding but no tongue."
 506) "    I will requite your loves. So, fare you well."
 507) "    Upon the platform, 'twixt eleven and twelve,"
 508) "    I'll visit you."
 509) "  All. Our duty to your honour.  "
 510) "  Ham. Your loves, as mine to you. Farewell."
 511) "                                        Exeunt [all but Hamlet]."
 512) "    My father's spirit- in arms? All is not well."
 513) "    I doubt some foul play. Would the night were come!"
 514) "    Till then sit still, my soul. Foul deeds will rise,"
 515) "    Though all the earth o'erwhelm them, to men's eyes."
 516) "Exit."
 517) "Scene III."
 518) "Elsinore. A room in the house of Polonius."
 519) "Enter Laertes and Ophelia."
 520) "  Laer. My necessaries are embark'd. Farewell."
 521) "    And, sister, as the winds give benefit"
 522) "    And convoy is assistant, do not sleep,"
 523) "    But let me hear from you."
 524) "  Oph. Do you doubt that?"
 525) "  Laer. For Hamlet, and the trifling of his favour,"
 526) "    Hold it a fashion, and a toy in blood;"
 527) "    A violet in the youth of primy nature,"
 528) "    Forward, not permanent- sweet, not lasting;"
 529) "    The perfume and suppliance of a minute;"
 530) "    No more."
 531) "  Oph. No more but so?"
 532) "  Laer. Think it no more."
 533) "    For nature crescent does not grow alone"
 534) "    In thews and bulk; but as this temple waxes,"
 535) "    The inward service of the mind and soul"
 536) "    Grows wide withal. Perhaps he loves you now,  "
 537) "    And now no soil nor cautel doth besmirch"
 538) "    The virtue of his will; but you must fear,"
 539) "    His greatness weigh'd, his will is not his own;"
 540) "    For he himself is subject to his birth."
 541) "    He may not, as unvalued persons do,"
 542) "    Carve for himself, for on his choice depends"
 543) "    The safety and health of this whole state,"
 544) "    And therefore must his choice be circumscrib'd"
 545) "    Unto the voice and yielding of that body"
 546) "    Whereof he is the head. Then if he says he loves you,"
 547) "    It fits your wisdom so far to believe it"
 548) "    As he in his particular act and place"
 549) "    May give his saying deed; which is no further"
 550) "    Than the main voice of Denmark goes withal."
 551) "    Then weigh what loss your honour may sustain"
 552) "    If with too credent ear you list his songs,"
 553) "    Or lose your heart, or your chaste treasure open"
 554) "    To his unmast'red importunity."
 555) "    Fear it, Ophelia, fear it, my dear sister,"
 556) "    And keep you in the rear of your affection,  "
 557) "    Out of the shot and danger of desire."
 558) "    The chariest maid is prodigal enough"
 559) "    If she unmask her beauty to the moon."
 560) "    Virtue itself scopes not calumnious strokes."
 561) "    The canker galls the infants of the spring"
 562) "    Too oft before their buttons be disclos'd,"
 563) "    And in the morn and liquid dew of youth"
 564) "    Contagious blastments are most imminent."
 565) "    Be wary then; best safety lies in fear."
 566) "    Youth to itself rebels, though none else near."
 567) "  Oph. I shall th' effect of this good lesson keep"
 568) "    As watchman to my heart. But, good my brother,"
 569) "    Do not as some ungracious pastors do,"
 570) "    Show me the steep and thorny way to heaven,"
 571) "    Whiles, like a puff'd and reckless libertine,"
 572) "    Himself the primrose path of dalliance treads"
 573) "    And recks not his own rede."
 574) "  Laer. O, fear me not!"
 575) "                       Enter Polonius.  "
 576) "    I stay too long. But here my father comes."
 577) "    A double blessing is a double grace;"
 578) "    Occasion smiles upon a second leave."
 579) "  Pol. Yet here, Laertes? Aboard, aboard, for shame!"
 580) "    The wind sits in the shoulder of your sail,"
 581) "    And you are stay'd for. There- my blessing with thee!"
 582) "    And these few precepts in thy memory"
 583) "    Look thou character. Give thy thoughts no tongue,"
 584) "    Nor any unproportion'd thought his act."
 585) "    Be thou familiar, but by no means vulgar:"
 586) "    Those friends thou hast, and their adoption tried,"
 587) "    Grapple them unto thy soul with hoops of steel;"
 588) "    But do not dull thy palm with entertainment"
 589) "    Of each new-hatch'd, unfledg'd comrade. Beware"
 590) "    Of entrance to a quarrel; but being in,"
 591) "    Bear't that th' opposed may beware of thee."
 592) "    Give every man thine ear, but few thy voice;"
 593) "    Take each man's censure, but reserve thy judgment."
 594) "    Costly thy habit as thy purse can buy,  "
 595) "    But not express'd in fancy; rich, not gaudy;"
 596) "    For the apparel oft proclaims the man,"
 597) "    And they in France of the best rank and station"
 598) "    Are most select and generous, chief in that."
 599) "    Neither a borrower nor a lender be;"
 600) "    For loan oft loses both itself and friend,"
 601) "    And borrowing dulls the edge of husbandry."
 602) "    This above all- to thine own self be true,"
 603) "    And it must follow, as the night the day,"
 604) "    Thou canst not then be false to any man."
 605) "    Farewell. My blessing season this in thee!"
 606) "  Laer. Most humbly do I take my leave, my lord."
 607) "  Pol. The time invites you. Go, your servants tend."
 608) "  Laer. Farewell, Ophelia, and remember well"
 609) "    What I have said to you."
 610) "  Oph. 'Tis in my memory lock'd,"
 611) "    And you yourself shall keep the key of it."
 612) "  Laer. Farewell.                                          Exit."
 613) "  Pol. What is't, Ophelia, he hath said to you?"
 614) "  Oph. So please you, something touching the Lord Hamlet.  "
 615) "  Pol. Marry, well bethought!"
 616) "    'Tis told me he hath very oft of late"
 617) "    Given private time to you, and you yourself"
 618) "    Have of your audience been most free and bounteous."
 619) "    If it be so- as so 'tis put on me,"
 620) "    And that in way of caution- I must tell you"
 621) "    You do not understand yourself so clearly"
 622) "    As it behooves my daughter and your honour."
 623) "    What is between you? Give me up the truth."
 624) "  Oph. He hath, my lord, of late made many tenders"
 625) "    Of his affection to me."
 626) "  Pol. Affection? Pooh! You speak like a green girl,"
 627) "    Unsifted in such perilous circumstance."
 628) "    Do you believe his tenders, as you call them?"
 629) "  Oph. I do not know, my lord, what I should think,"
 630) "  Pol. Marry, I will teach you! Think yourself a baby"
 631) "    That you have ta'en these tenders for true pay,"
 632) "    Which are not sterling. Tender yourself more dearly,"
 633) "    Or (not to crack the wind of the poor phrase,"
 634) "    Running it thus) you'll tender me a fool.  "
 635) "  Oph. My lord, he hath importun'd me with love"
 636) "    In honourable fashion."
 637) "  Pol. Ay, fashion you may call it. Go to, go to!"
 638) "  Oph. And hath given countenance to his speech, my lord,"
 639) "    With almost all the holy vows of heaven."
 640) "  Pol. Ay, springes to catch woodcocks! I do know,"
 641) "    When the blood burns, how prodigal the soul"
 642) "    Lends the tongue vows. These blazes, daughter,"
 643) "    Giving more light than heat, extinct in both"
 644) "    Even in their promise, as it is a-making,"
 645) "    You must not take for fire. From this time"
 646) "    Be something scanter of your maiden presence."
 647) "    Set your entreatments at a higher rate"
 648) "    Than a command to parley. For Lord Hamlet,"
 649) "    Believe so much in him, that he is young,"
 650) "    And with a larger tether may he walk"
 651) "    Than may be given you. In few, Ophelia,"
 652) "    Do not believe his vows; for they are brokers,"
 653) "    Not of that dye which their investments show,"
 654) "    But mere implorators of unholy suits,  "
 655) "    Breathing like sanctified and pious bawds,"
 656) "    The better to beguile. This is for all:"
 657) "    I would not, in plain terms, from this time forth"
 658) "    Have you so slander any moment leisure"
 659) "    As to give words or talk with the Lord Hamlet."
 660) "    Look to't, I charge you. Come your ways."
 661) "  Oph. I shall obey, my lord."
 662) "                                                         Exeunt."
 663) "Scene IV."
 664) "Elsinore. The platform before the Castle."
 665) "Enter Hamlet, Horatio, and Marcellus."
 666) "  Ham. The air bites shrewdly; it is very cold."
 667) "  Hor. It is a nipping and an eager air."
 668) "  Ham. What hour now?"
 669) "  Hor. I think it lacks of twelve."
 670) "  Mar. No, it is struck."
 671) "  Hor. Indeed? I heard it not. It then draws near the season"
 672) "    Wherein the spirit held his wont to walk."
 673) "                   A flourish of trumpets, and two pieces go off."
 674) "    What does this mean, my lord?"
 675) "  Ham. The King doth wake to-night and takes his rouse,"
 676) "    Keeps wassail, and the swagg'ring upspring reels,"
 677) "    And, as he drains his draughts of Rhenish down,"
 678) "    The kettledrum and trumpet thus bray out"
 679) "    The triumph of his pledge."
 680) "  Hor. Is it a custom?"
 681) "  Ham. Ay, marry, is't;"
 682) "    But to my mind, though I am native here  "
 683) "    And to the manner born, it is a custom"
 684) "    More honour'd in the breach than the observance."
 685) "    This heavy-headed revel east and west"
 686) "    Makes us traduc'd and tax'd of other nations;"
 687) "    They clip us drunkards and with swinish phrase"
 688) "    Soil our addition; and indeed it takes"
 689) "    From our achievements, though perform'd at height,"
 690) "    The pith and marrow of our attribute."
 691) "    So oft it chances in particular men"
 692) "    That, for some vicious mole of nature in them,"
 693) "    As in their birth,- wherein they are not guilty,"
 694) "    Since nature cannot choose his origin,-"
 695) "    By the o'ergrowth of some complexion,"
 696) "    Oft breaking down the pales and forts of reason,"
 697) "    Or by some habit that too much o'erleavens"
 698) "    The form of plausive manners, that these men"
 699) "    Carrying, I say, the stamp of one defect,"
 700) "    Being nature's livery, or fortune's star,"
 701) "    Their virtues else- be they as pure as grace,"
 702) "    As infinite as man may undergo-  "
 703) "    Shall in the general censure take corruption"
 704) "    From that particular fault. The dram of e'il"
 705) "    Doth all the noble substance often dout To his own scandal."
 706) "                         Enter Ghost."
 707) "  Hor. Look, my lord, it comes!"
 708) "  Ham. Angels and ministers of grace defend us!"
 709) "    Be thou a spirit of health or goblin damn'd,"
 710) "    Bring with thee airs from heaven or blasts from hell,"
 711) "    Be thy intents wicked or charitable,"
 712) "    Thou com'st in such a questionable shape"
 713) "    That I will speak to thee. I'll call thee Hamlet,"
 714) "    King, father, royal Dane. O, answer me?"
 715) "    Let me not burst in ignorance, but tell"
 716) "    Why thy canoniz'd bones, hearsed in death,"
 717) "    Have burst their cerements; why the sepulchre"
 718) "    Wherein we saw thee quietly inurn'd,"
 719) "    Hath op'd his ponderous and marble jaws"
 720) "    To cast thee up again. What may this mean  "
 721) "    That thou, dead corse, again in complete steel,"
 722) "    Revisits thus the glimpses of the moon,"
 723) "    Making night hideous, and we fools of nature"
 724) "    So horridly to shake our disposition"
 725) "    With thoughts beyond the reaches of our souls?"
 726) "    Say, why is this? wherefore? What should we do?"
 727) "                                           Ghost beckons Hamlet."
 728) "  Hor. It beckons you to go away with it,"
 729) "    As if it some impartment did desire"
 730) "    To you alone."
 731) "  Mar. Look with what courteous action"
 732) "    It waves you to a more removed ground."
 733) "    But do not go with it!"
 734) "  Hor. No, by no means!"
 735) "  Ham. It will not speak. Then will I follow it."
 736) "  Hor. Do not, my lord!"
 737) "  Ham. Why, what should be the fear?"
 738) "    I do not set my life at a pin's fee;"
 739) "    And for my soul, what can it do to that,"
 740) "    Being a thing immortal as itself?  "
 741) "    It waves me forth again. I'll follow it."
 742) "  Hor. What if it tempt you toward the flood, my lord,"
 743) "    Or to the dreadful summit of the cliff"
 744) "    That beetles o'er his base into the sea,"
 745) "    And there assume some other, horrible form"
 746) "    Which might deprive your sovereignty of reason"
 747) "    And draw you into madness? Think of it."
 748) "    The very place puts toys of desperation,"
 749) "    Without more motive, into every brain"
 750) "    That looks so many fadoms to the sea"
 751) "    And hears it roar beneath."
 752) "  Ham. It waves me still."
 753) "    Go on. I'll follow thee."
 754) "  Mar. You shall not go, my lord."
 755) "  Ham. Hold off your hands!"
 756) "  Hor. Be rul'd. You shall not go."
 757) "  Ham. My fate cries out"
 758) "    And makes each petty artire in this body"
 759) "    As hardy as the Nemean lion's nerve."
 760) "                                                [Ghost beckons.]  "
 761) "    Still am I call'd. Unhand me, gentlemen."
 762) "    By heaven, I'll make a ghost of him that lets me!-"
 763) "    I say, away!- Go on. I'll follow thee."
 764) "                                        Exeunt Ghost and Hamlet."
 765) "  Hor. He waxes desperate with imagination."
 766) "  Mar. Let's follow. 'Tis not fit thus to obey him."
 767) "  Hor. Have after. To what issue wail this come?"
 768) "  Mar. Something is rotten in the state of Denmark."
 769) "  Hor. Heaven will direct it."
 770) "  Mar. Nay, let's follow him."
 771) "                                                         Exeunt."
 772) "Scene V."
 773) "Elsinore. The Castle. Another part of the fortifications."
 774) "Enter Ghost and Hamlet."
 775) "  Ham. Whither wilt thou lead me? Speak! I'll go no further."
 776) "  Ghost. Mark me."
 777) "  Ham. I will."
 778) "  Ghost. My hour is almost come,"
 779) "    When I to sulph'rous and tormenting flames"
 780) "    Must render up myself."
 781) "  Ham. Alas, poor ghost!"
 782) "  Ghost. Pity me not, but lend thy serious hearing"
 783) "    To what I shall unfold."
 784) "  Ham. Speak. I am bound to hear."
 785) "  Ghost. So art thou to revenge, when thou shalt hear."
 786) "  Ham. What?"
 787) "  Ghost. I am thy father's spirit,"
 788) "    Doom'd for a certain term to walk the night,"
 789) "    And for the day confin'd to fast in fires,"
 790) "    Till the foul crimes done in my days of nature"
 791) "    Are burnt and purg'd away. But that I am forbid  "
 792) "    To tell the secrets of my prison house,"
 793) "    I could a tale unfold whose lightest word"
 794) "    Would harrow up thy soul, freeze thy young blood,"
 795) "    Make thy two eyes, like stars, start from their spheres,"
 796) "    Thy knotted and combined locks to part,"
 797) "    And each particular hair to stand an end"
 798) "    Like quills upon the fretful porpentine."
 799) "    But this eternal blazon must not be"
 800) "    To ears of flesh and blood. List, list, O, list!"
 801) "    If thou didst ever thy dear father love-"
 802) "  Ham. O God!"
 803) "  Ghost. Revenge his foul and most unnatural murther."
 804) "  Ham. Murther?"
 805) "  Ghost. Murther most foul, as in the best it is;"
 806) "    But this most foul, strange, and unnatural."
 807) "  Ham. Haste me to know't, that I, with wings as swift"
 808) "    As meditation or the thoughts of love,"
 809) "    May sweep to my revenge."
 810) "  Ghost. I find thee apt;"
 811) "    And duller shouldst thou be than the fat weed  "
 812) "    That rots itself in ease on Lethe wharf,"
 813) "    Wouldst thou not stir in this. Now, Hamlet, hear."
 814) "    'Tis given out that, sleeping in my orchard,"
 815) "    A serpent stung me. So the whole ear of Denmark"
 816) "    Is by a forged process of my death"
 817) "    Rankly abus'd. But know, thou noble youth,"
 818) "    The serpent that did sting thy father's life"
 819) "    Now wears his crown."
 820) "  Ham. O my prophetic soul!"
 821) "    My uncle?"
 822) "  Ghost. Ay, that incestuous, that adulterate beast,"
 823) "    With witchcraft of his wit, with traitorous gifts-"
 824) "    O wicked wit and gifts, that have the power"
 825) "    So to seduce!- won to his shameful lust"
 826) "    The will of my most seeming-virtuous queen."
 827) "    O Hamlet, what a falling-off was there,"
 828) "    From me, whose love was of that dignity"
 829) "    That it went hand in hand even with the vow"
 830) "    I made to her in marriage, and to decline"
 831) "    Upon a wretch whose natural gifts were poor  "
 832) "    To those of mine!"
 833) "    But virtue, as it never will be mov'd,"
 834) "    Though lewdness court it in a shape of heaven,"
 835) "    So lust, though to a radiant angel link'd,"
 836) "    Will sate itself in a celestial bed"
 837) "    And prey on garbage."
 838) "    But soft! methinks I scent the morning air."
 839) "    Brief let me be. Sleeping within my orchard,"
 840) "    My custom always of the afternoon,"
 841) "    Upon my secure hour thy uncle stole,"
 842) "    With juice of cursed hebona in a vial,"
 843) "    And in the porches of my ears did pour"
 844) "    The leperous distilment; whose effect"
 845) "    Holds such an enmity with blood of man"
 846) "    That swift as quicksilverr it courses through"
 847) "    The natural gates and alleys of the body,"
 848) "    And with a sudden vigour it doth posset"
 849) "    And curd, like eager droppings into milk,"
 850) "    The thin and wholesome blood. So did it mine;"
 851) "    And a most instant tetter bark'd about,  "
 852) "    Most lazar-like, with vile and loathsome crust"
 853) "    All my smooth body."
 854) "    Thus was I, sleeping, by a brother's hand"
 855) "    Of life, of crown, of queen, at once dispatch'd;"
 856) "    Cut off even in the blossoms of my sin,"
 857) "    Unhous'led, disappointed, unanel'd,"
 858) "    No reckoning made, but sent to my account"
 859) "    With all my imperfections on my head."
 860) "  Ham. O, horrible! O, horrible! most horrible!"
 861) "  Ghost. If thou hast nature in thee, bear it not."
 862) "    Let not the royal bed of Denmark be"
 863) "    A couch for luxury and damned incest."
 864) "    But, howsoever thou pursuest this act,"
 865) "    Taint not thy mind, nor let thy soul contrive"
 866) "    Against thy mother aught. Leave her to heaven,"
 867) "    And to those thorns that in her bosom lodge"
 868) "    To prick and sting her. Fare thee well at once."
 869) "    The glowworm shows the matin to be near"
 870) "    And gins to pale his uneffectual fire."
 871) "    Adieu, adieu, adieu! Remember me.                      Exit.  "
 872) "  Ham. O all you host of heaven! O earth! What else?"
 873) "    And shall I couple hell? Hold, hold, my heart!"
 874) "    And you, my sinews, grow not instant old,"
 875) "    But bear me stiffly up. Remember thee?"
 876) "    Ay, thou poor ghost, while memory holds a seat"
 877) "    In this distracted globe. Remember thee?"
 878) "    Yea, from the table of my memory"
 879) "    I'll wipe away all trivial fond records,"
 880) "    All saws of books, all forms, all pressures past"
 881) "    That youth and observation copied there,"
 882) "    And thy commandment all alone shall live"
 883) "    Within the book and volume of my brain,"
 884) "    Unmix'd with baser matter. Yes, by heaven!"
 885) "    O most pernicious woman!"
 886) "    O villain, villain, smiling, damned villain!"
 887) "    My tables! Meet it is I set it down"
 888) "    That one may smile, and smile, and be a villain;"
 889) "    At least I am sure it may be so in Denmark.        [Writes.]"
 890) "    So, uncle, there you are. Now to my word:"
 891) "    It is 'Adieu, adieu! Remember me.'  "
 892) "    I have sworn't."
 893) "  Hor. (within) My lord, my lord!"
 894) "                   Enter Horatio and Marcellus."
 895) "  Mar. Lord Hamlet!"
 896) "  Hor. Heaven secure him!"
 897) "  Ham. So be it!"
 898) "  Mar. Illo, ho, ho, my lord!"
 899) "  Ham. Hillo, ho, ho, boy! Come, bird, come."
 900) "  Mar. How is't, my noble lord?"
 901) "  Hor. What news, my lord?"
 902) "  Mar. O, wonderful!"
 903) "  Hor. Good my lord, tell it."
 904) "  Ham. No, you will reveal it."
 905) "  Hor. Not I, my lord, by heaven!"
 906) "  Mar. Nor I, my lord."
 907) "  Ham. How say you then? Would heart of man once think it?"
 908) "    But you'll be secret?"
 909) "  Both. Ay, by heaven, my lord.  "
 910) "  Ham. There's neer a villain dwelling in all Denmark"
 911) "    But he's an arrant knave."
 912) "  Hor. There needs no ghost, my lord, come from the grave"
 913) "    To tell us this."
 914) "  Ham. Why, right! You are in the right!"
 915) "    And so, without more circumstance at all,"
 916) "    I hold it fit that we shake hands and part;"
 917) "    You, as your business and desires shall point you,"
 918) "    For every man hath business and desire,"
 919) "    Such as it is; and for my own poor part,"
 920) "    Look you, I'll go pray."
 921) "  Hor. These are but wild and whirling words, my lord."
 922) "  Ham. I am sorry they offend you, heartily;"
 923) "    Yes, faith, heartily."
 924) "  Hor. There's no offence, my lord."
 925) "  Ham. Yes, by Saint Patrick, but there is, Horatio,"
 926) "    And much offence too. Touching this vision here,"
 927) "    It is an honest ghost, that let me tell you."
 928) "    For your desire to know what is between us,"
 929) "    O'ermaster't as you may. And now, good friends,  "
 930) "    As you are friends, scholars, and soldiers,"
 931) "    Give me one poor request."
 932) "  Hor. What is't, my lord? We will."
 933) "  Ham. Never make known what you have seen to-night."
 934) "  Both. My lord, we will not."
 935) "  Ham. Nay, but swear't."
 936) "  Hor. In faith,"
 937) "    My lord, not I."
 938) "  Mar. Nor I, my lord- in faith."
 939) "  Ham. Upon my sword."
 940) "  Mar. We have sworn, my lord, already."
 941) "  Ham. Indeed, upon my sword, indeed."
 942) "                 Ghost cries under the stage."
 943) "  Ghost. Swear."
 944) "  Ham. Aha boy, say'st thou so? Art thou there, truepenny?"
 945) "    Come on! You hear this fellow in the cellarage."
 946) "    Consent to swear."
 947) "  Hor. Propose the oath, my lord.  "
 948) "  Ham. Never to speak of this that you have seen."
 949) "    Swear by my sword."
 950) "  Ghost. [beneath] Swear."
 951) "  Ham. Hic et ubique? Then we'll shift our ground."
 952) "    Come hither, gentlemen,"
 953) "    And lay your hands again upon my sword."
 954) "    Never to speak of this that you have heard:"
 955) "    Swear by my sword."
 956) "  Ghost. [beneath] Swear by his sword."
 957) "  Ham. Well said, old mole! Canst work i' th' earth so fast?"
 958) "    A worthy pioner! Once more remove, good friends.""
 959) "  Hor. O day and night, but this is wondrous strange!"
 960) "  Ham. And therefore as a stranger give it welcome."
 961) "    There are more things in heaven and earth, Horatio,"
 962) "    Than are dreamt of in your philosophy."
 963) "    But come!"
 964) "    Here, as before, never, so help you mercy,"
 965) "    How strange or odd soe'er I bear myself"
 966) "    (As I perchance hereafter shall think meet"
 967) "    To put an antic disposition on),  "
 968) "    That you, at such times seeing me, never shall,"
 969) "    With arms encumb'red thus, or this head-shake,"
 970) "    Or by pronouncing of some doubtful phrase,"
 971) "    As 'Well, well, we know,' or 'We could, an if we would,'"
 972) "    Or 'If we list to speak,' or 'There be, an if they might,'"
 973) "    Or such ambiguous giving out, to note"
 974) "    That you know aught of me- this is not to do,"
 975) "    So grace and mercy at your most need help you,"
 976) "    Swear."
 977) "  Ghost. [beneath] Swear."
 978) "                                                   [They swear.]"
 979) "  Ham. Rest, rest, perturbed spirit! So, gentlemen,"
 980) "    With all my love I do commend me to you;"
 981) "    And what so poor a man as Hamlet is"
 982) "    May do t' express his love and friending to you,"
 983) "    God willing, shall not lack. Let us go in together;"
 984) "    And still your fingers on your lips, I pray."
 985) "    The time is out of joint. O cursed spite"
 986) "    That ever I was born to set it right!"
 987) "    Nay, come, let's go together.  "
 988) "                                                         Exeunt."
 989) "Act II. Scene I."
 990) "Elsinore. A room in the house of Polonius."
 991) "Enter Polonius and Reynaldo."
 992) "  Pol. Give him this money and these notes, Reynaldo."
 993) "  Rey. I will, my lord."
 994) "  Pol. You shall do marvell's wisely, good Reynaldo,"
 995) "    Before You visit him, to make inquire"
 996) "    Of his behaviour."
 997) "  Rey. My lord, I did intend it."
 998) "  Pol. Marry, well said, very well said. Look you, sir,"
 999) "    Enquire me first what Danskers are in Paris;"
1000) "    And how, and who, what means, and where they keep,"
1001) "    What company, at what expense; and finding"
1002) "    By this encompassment and drift of question"
1003) "    That they do know my son, come you more nearer"
1004) "    Than your particular demands will touch it."
1005) "    Take you, as 'twere, some distant knowledge of him;"
1006) "    As thus, 'I know his father and his friends,"
1007) "    And in part him.' Do you mark this, Reynaldo?"
1008) "  Rey. Ay, very well, my lord.  "
1009) "  Pol. 'And in part him, but,' you may say, 'not well."
1010) "    But if't be he I mean, he's very wild"
1011) "    Addicted so and so'; and there put on him"
1012) "    What forgeries you please; marry, none so rank"
1013) "    As may dishonour him- take heed of that;"
1014) "    But, sir, such wanton, wild, and usual slips"
1015) "    As are companions noted and most known"
1016) "    To youth and liberty."
1017) "  Rey. As gaming, my lord."
1018) "  Pol. Ay, or drinking, fencing, swearing, quarrelling,"
1019) "    Drabbing. You may go so far."
1020) "  Rey. My lord, that would dishonour him."
1021) "  Pol. Faith, no, as you may season it in the charge."
1022) "    You must not put another scandal on him,"
1023) "    That he is open to incontinency."
1024) "    That's not my meaning. But breathe his faults so quaintly"
1025) "    That they may seem the taints of liberty,"
1026) "    The flash and outbreak of a fiery mind,"
1027) "    A savageness in unreclaimed blood,"
1028) "    Of general assault.  "
1029) "  Rey. But, my good lord-"
1030) "  Pol. Wherefore should you do this?"
1031) "  Rey. Ay, my lord,"
1032) "    I would know that."
1033) "  Pol. Marry, sir, here's my drift,"
1034) "    And I believe it is a fetch of warrant."
1035) "    You laying these slight sullies on my son"
1036) "    As 'twere a thing a little soil'd i' th' working,"
1037) "    Mark you,"
1038) "    Your party in converse, him you would sound,"
1039) "    Having ever seen in the prenominate crimes"
1040) "    The youth you breathe of guilty, be assur'd"
1041) "    He closes with you in this consequence:"
1042) "    'Good sir,' or so, or 'friend,' or 'gentleman'-"
1043) "    According to the phrase or the addition"
1044) "    Of man and country-"
1045) "  Rey. Very good, my lord."
1046) "  Pol. And then, sir, does 'a this- 'a does- What was I about to say?"
1047) "    By the mass, I was about to say something! Where did I leave?"
1048) "  Rey. At 'closes in the consequence,' at 'friend or so,' and  "
1049) "    gentleman.'"
1050) "  Pol. At 'closes in the consequence'- Ay, marry!"
1051) "    He closes thus: 'I know the gentleman."
1052) "    I saw him yesterday, or t'other day,"
1053) "    Or then, or then, with such or such; and, as you say,"
1054) "    There was 'a gaming; there o'ertook in's rouse;"
1055) "    There falling out at tennis'; or perchance,"
1056) "    'I saw him enter such a house of sale,'"
1057) "    Videlicet, a brothel, or so forth."
1058) "    See you now-"
1059) "    Your bait of falsehood takes this carp of truth;"
1060) "    And thus do we of wisdom and of reach,"
1061) "    With windlasses and with assays of bias,"
1062) "    By indirections find directions out."
1063) "    So, by my former lecture and advice,"
1064) "    Shall you my son. You have me, have you not"
1065) "  Rey. My lord, I have."
1066) "  Pol. God b' wi' ye, fare ye well!"
1067) "  Rey. Good my lord!                                    [Going.]"
1068) "  Pol. Observe his inclination in yourself.  "
1069) "  Rey. I shall, my lord."
1070) "  Pol. And let him ply his music."
1071) "  Rey. Well, my lord."
1072) "  Pol. Farewell!"
1073) "                                                  Exit Reynaldo."
1074) "                       Enter Ophelia."
1075) "    How now, Ophelia? What's the matter?"
1076) "  Oph. O my lord, my lord, I have been so affrighted!"
1077) "  Pol. With what, i' th' name of God I"
1078) "  Oph. My lord, as I was sewing in my closet,"
1079) "    Lord Hamlet, with his doublet all unbrac'd,"
1080) "    No hat upon his head, his stockings foul'd,"
1081) "    Ungart'red, and down-gyved to his ankle;"
1082) "    Pale as his shirt, his knees knocking each other,"
1083) "    And with a look so piteous in purport"
1084) "    As if he had been loosed out of hell"
1085) "    To speak of horrors- he comes before me."
1086) "  Pol. Mad for thy love?  "
1087) "  Oph. My lord, I do not know,"
1088) "    But truly I do fear it."
1089) "  Pol. What said he?"
1090) "  Oph. He took me by the wrist and held me hard;"
1091) "    Then goes he to the length of all his arm,"
1092) "    And, with his other hand thus o'er his brow,"
1093) "    He falls to such perusal of my face"
1094) "    As he would draw it. Long stay'd he so."
1095) "    At last, a little shaking of mine arm,"
1096) "    And thrice his head thus waving up and down,"
1097) "    He rais'd a sigh so piteous and profound"
1098) "    As it did seem to shatter all his bulk"
1099) "    And end his being. That done, he lets me go,"
1100) "    And with his head over his shoulder turn'd"
1101) "    He seem'd to find his way without his eyes,"
1102) "    For out o' doors he went without their help"
1103) "    And to the last bended their light on me."
1104) "  Pol. Come, go with me. I will go seek the King."
1105) "    This is the very ecstasy of love,"
1106) "    Whose violent property fordoes itself  "
1107) "    And leads the will to desperate undertakings"
1108) "    As oft as any passion under heaven"
1109) "    That does afflict our natures. I am sorry."
1110) "    What, have you given him any hard words of late?"
1111) "  Oph. No, my good lord; but, as you did command,"
1112) "    I did repel his letters and denied"
1113) "    His access to me."
1114) "  Pol. That hath made him mad."
1115) "    I am sorry that with better heed and judgment"
1116) "    I had not quoted him. I fear'd he did but trifle"
1117) "    And meant to wrack thee; but beshrew my jealousy!"
1118) "    By heaven, it is as proper to our age"
1119) "    To cast beyond ourselves in our opinions"
1120) "    As it is common for the younger sort"
1121) "    To lack discretion. Come, go we to the King."
1122) "    This must be known; which, being kept close, might move"
1123) "    More grief to hide than hate to utter love."
1124) "    Come."

1130) "    Moreover that we much did long to see you,"

1177) "  Pol. Have I, my lord? Assure you, my good liege,"

1195) "    Welcome, my good friends."
1196) "    Say, Voltemand, what from our brother Norway?"
1197) "  Volt. Most fair return of greetings and desires."
1198) "    Upon our first, he sent out to suppress"
1199) "    His nephew's levies; which to him appear'd"
1200) "    To be a preparation 'gainst the Polack,  "

1225) "  Pol. This business is well ended."
1226) "    My liege, and madam, to expostulate"
1227) "    What majesty should be, what duty is,"
1228) "    Why day is day, night is night, and time is time."
1229) "    Were nothing but to waste night, day, and time."
1230) "    Therefore, since brevity is the soul of wit,"
1231) "    And tediousness the limbs and outward flourishes,"
1232) "    I will be brief. Your noble son is mad."
1233) "    Mad call I it; for, to define true madness,"
1234) "    What is't but to be nothing else but mad?"
1235) "    But let that go."
1236) "  Queen. More matter, with less art."
1237) "  Pol. Madam, I swear I use no art at all."
1238) "    That he is mad, 'tis true: 'tis true 'tis pity;"
1239) "    And pity 'tis 'tis true. A foolish figure!"
1240) "    But farewell it, for I will use no art.  "

1243) "    Or rather say, the cause of this defect,"

1245) "    Thus it remains, and the remainder thus."

1453) "  Ros. My lord, there was no such stuff in my thoughts.  "

1455) "  Ros. To think, my lord, if you delight not in man, what lenten"
1456) "    entertainment the players shall receive from you. We coted them"
1457) "    on the way, and hither are they coming to offer you service."
1458) "  Ham. He that plays the king shall be welcome- his Majesty shall"

1461) "    end his part in peace; the clown shall make those laugh whose"

1463) "    freely, or the blank verse shall halt fort. What players are"

1473) "  Ros. No indeed are they not.  "

1479) "    them) that many wearing rapiers are afraid of goosequills and"

1484) "    themselves to common players (as it is most like, if their means"

1487) "  Ros. Faith, there has been much to do on both sides; and the nation"

1491) "  Ham. Is't possible?"
