1534) "  Ham. O Jephthah, judge of Israel, what a treasure hadst thou!"
1535) "  Pol. What treasure had he, my lord?"
1536) "  Ham. Why,"
1537) "         'One fair daughter, and no more,"
1538) "           The which he loved passing well.'"
1539) "  Pol. [aside] Still on my daughter."
1540) "  Ham. Am I not i' th' right, old Jephthah?"
1541) "  Pol. If you call me Jephthah, my lord, I have a daughter that I"
1542) "    love passing well."
1543) "  Ham. Nay, that follows not."
1544) "  Pol. What follows then, my lord?"
1545) "  Ham. Why,"
1546) "           'As by lot, God wot,'"
1547) " and then, you know,"
1548) "  "
1549) "           'It came to pass, as most like it was.'"
1550) "    The first row of the pious chanson will show you more; for look"
1551) "    where my abridgment comes."
1552) "                     Enter four or five Players."
1553) "    You are welcome, masters; welcome, all.- I am glad to see thee"
1554) "    well.- Welcome, good friends.- O, my old friend? Why, thy face is"
1555) "    valanc'd since I saw thee last. Com'st' thou to' beard me in"
1556) "    Denmark?- What, my young lady and mistress? By'r Lady, your"
1557) "    ladyship is nearer to heaven than when I saw you last by the"
1558) "    altitude of a chopine. Pray God your voice, like a piece of"
1559) "    uncurrent gold, be not crack'd within the ring.- Masters, you are"
1560) "    all welcome. We'll e'en to't like French falconers, fly at"
1561) "    anything we see. We'll have a speech straight. Come, give us a"
1562) "    taste of your quality. Come, a passionate speech."
1563) "  1. Play. What speech, my good lord?"
1564) "  Ham. I heard thee speak me a speech once, but it was never acted;"
1565) "    or if it was, not above once; for the play, I remember, pleas'd  "
1566) "    not the million, 'twas caviary to the general; but it was (as I"
1567) "    receiv'd it, and others, whose judgments in such matters cried in"
1568) "    the top of mine) an excellent play, well digested in the scenes,"
1569) "    set down with as much modesty as cunning. I remember one said"
1570) "    there were no sallets in the lines to make the matter savoury,"
1571) "    nor no matter in the phrase that might indict the author of"
1572) "    affectation; but call'd it an honest method, as wholesome as"
1573) "    sweet, and by very much more handsome than fine. One speech in't"
1574) "    I chiefly lov'd. 'Twas AEneas' tale to Dido, and thereabout of it"
1575) "    especially where he speaks of Priam's slaughter. If it live in"
1576) "    your memory, begin at this line- let me see, let me see:"
1577) "         'The rugged Pyrrhus, like th' Hyrcanian beast-'"
1578) "    'Tis not so; it begins with Pyrrhus:"
1579) "         'The rugged Pyrrhus, he whose sable arms,"
1580) "         Black as his purpose, did the night resemble"
1581) "         When he lay couched in the ominous horse,"
1582) "         Hath now this dread and black complexion smear'd  "
1583) "         With heraldry more dismal. Head to foot"
1584) "         Now is be total gules, horridly trick'd"
1585) "         With blood of fathers, mothers, daughters, sons,"
1586) "         Bak'd and impasted with the parching streets,"
1587) "         That lend a tyrannous and a damned light"
1588) "         To their lord's murther. Roasted in wrath and fire,"
1589) "         And thus o'ersized with coagulate gore,"
1590) "         With eyes like carbuncles, the hellish Pyrrhus"
1591) "         Old grandsire Priam seeks.'"
1592) "    So, proceed you."
1593) "  Pol. Fore God, my lord, well spoken, with good accent and good"
1594) "     discretion."
1595) "  1. Play. 'Anon he finds him,"
1596) "      Striking too short at Greeks. His antique sword,"
1597) "      Rebellious to his arm, lies where it falls,"
1598) "      Repugnant to command. Unequal match'd,"
1599) "      Pyrrhus at Priam drives, in rage strikes wide;"
1600) "      But with the whiff and wind of his fell sword  "
1601) "      Th' unnerved father falls. Then senseless Ilium,"
1602) "      Seeming to feel this blow, with flaming top"
1603) "      Stoops to his base, and with a hideous crash"
1604) "      Takes prisoner Pyrrhus' ear. For lo! his sword,"
1605) "      Which was declining on the milky head"
1606) "      Of reverend Priam, seem'd i' th' air to stick."
1607) "      So, as a painted tyrant, Pyrrhus stood,"
1608) "      And, like a neutral to his will and matter,"
1609) "      Did nothing."
1610) "      But, as we often see, against some storm,"
1611) "      A silence in the heavens, the rack stand still,"
1612) "      The bold winds speechless, and the orb below"
1613) "      As hush as death- anon the dreadful thunder"
1614) "      Doth rend the region; so, after Pyrrhus' pause,"
1615) "      Aroused vengeance sets him new awork;"
1616) "      And never did the Cyclops' hammers fall"
1617) "      On Mars's armour, forg'd for proof eterne,"
1618) "      With less remorse than Pyrrhus' bleeding sword"
1619) "      Now falls on Priam."
1620) "      Out, out, thou strumpet Fortune! All you gods,  "
1621) "      In general synod take away her power;"
1622) "      Break all the spokes and fellies from her wheel,"
1623) "      And bowl the round nave down the hill of heaven,"
1624) "      As low as to the fiends!"
1625) "  Pol. This is too long."
1626) "  Ham. It shall to the barber's, with your beard.- Prithee say on."
1627) "    He's for a jig or a tale of bawdry, or he sleeps. Say on; come to"
1628) "    Hecuba."
1629) "  1. Play. 'But who, O who, had seen the mobled queen-'"
1630) "  Ham. 'The mobled queen'?"
1631) "  Pol. That's good! 'Mobled queen' is good."
1632) "  1. Play. 'Run barefoot up and down, threat'ning the flames"
1633) "      With bisson rheum; a clout upon that head"
1634) "      Where late the diadem stood, and for a robe,"
1635) "      About her lank and all o'erteemed loins,"
1636) "      A blanket, in the alarm of fear caught up-  "
1637) "      Who this had seen, with tongue in venom steep'd"
1638) "      'Gainst Fortune's state would treason have pronounc'd."
1639) "      But if the gods themselves did see her then,"
1640) "      When she saw Pyrrhus make malicious sport"
1641) "      In Mincing with his sword her husband's limbs,"
1642) "      The instant burst of clamour that she made"
1643) "      (Unless things mortal move them not at all)"
1644) "      Would have made milch the burning eyes of heaven"
1645) "      And passion in the gods.'"
1646) "  Pol. Look, whe'r he has not turn'd his colour, and has tears in's"
1647) "    eyes. Prithee no more!"
1648) "  Ham. 'Tis well. I'll have thee speak out the rest of this soon.-"
1649) "    Good my lord, will you see the players well bestow'd? Do you"
1650) "    hear? Let them be well us'd; for they are the abstract and brief"
1651) "    chronicles of the time. After your death you were better have a"
1652) "    bad epitaph than their ill report while you live."
1653) "  Pol. My lord, I will use them according to their desert."
1654) "  Ham. God's bodykins, man, much better! Use every man after his"
1655) "    desert, and who should scape whipping? Use them after your own  "
1656) "    honour and dignity. The less they deserve, the more merit is in"
1657) "    your bounty. Take them in."
1658) "  Pol. Come, sirs."
1659) "  Ham. Follow him, friends. We'll hear a play to-morrow."
1660) "                 Exeunt Polonius and Players [except the First]."
1661) "    Dost thou hear me, old friend? Can you play 'The Murther of"
1662) "    Gonzago'?"
1663) "  1. Play. Ay, my lord."
1664) "  Ham. We'll ha't to-morrow night. You could, for a need, study a"
1665) "    speech of some dozen or sixteen lines which I would set down and"
1666) "    insert in't, could you not?"
1667) "  1. Play. Ay, my lord."
1668) "  Ham. Very well. Follow that lord- and look you mock him not."
1669) "                                            [Exit First Player.]"
1670) "    My good friends, I'll leave you till night. You are welcome to"
1671) "    Elsinore."
1672) "  Ros. Good my lord!"
1673) "  Ham. Ay, so, God b' wi' ye!"
1674) "                            [Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern"
1675) "    Now I am alone.  "
1676) "    O what a rogue and peasant slave am I!"
1677) "    Is it not monstrous that this player here,"
1678) "    But in a fiction, in a dream of passion,"
1679) "    Could force his soul so to his own conceit"
1680) "    That, from her working, all his visage wann'd,"
1681) "    Tears in his eyes, distraction in's aspect,"
1682) "    A broken voice, and his whole function suiting"
1683) "    With forms to his conceit? And all for nothing!"
1684) "    For Hecuba!"
1685) "    What's Hecuba to him, or he to Hecuba,"
1686) "    That he should weep for her? What would he do,"
1687) "    Had he the motive and the cue for passion"
1688) "    That I have? He would drown the stage with tears"
1689) "    And cleave the general ear with horrid speech;"
1690) "    Make mad the guilty and appal the free,"
1691) "    Confound the ignorant, and amaze indeed"
1692) "    The very faculties of eyes and ears."
1693) "    Yet I,"
1694) "    A dull and muddy-mettled rascal, peak"
1695) "    Like John-a-dreams, unpregnant of my cause,  "
1696) "    And can say nothing! No, not for a king,"
1697) "    Upon whose property and most dear life"
1698) "    A damn'd defeat was made. Am I a coward?"
1699) "    Who calls me villain? breaks my pate across?"
1700) "    Plucks off my beard and blows it in my face?"
1701) "    Tweaks me by th' nose? gives me the lie i' th' throat"
1702) "    As deep as to the lungs? Who does me this, ha?"
1703) "    'Swounds, I should take it! for it cannot be"
1704) "    But I am pigeon-liver'd and lack gall"
1705) "    To make oppression bitter, or ere this"
1706) "    I should have fatted all the region kites"
1707) "    With this slave's offal. Bloody bawdy villain!"
1708) "    Remorseless, treacherous, lecherous, kindless villain!"
1709) "    O, vengeance!"
1710) "    Why, what an ass am I! This is most brave,"
1711) "    That I, the son of a dear father murther'd,"
1712) "    Prompted to my revenge by heaven and hell,"
1713) "    Must (like a whore) unpack my heart with words"
1714) "    And fall a-cursing like a very drab,"
1715) "    A scullion!  "
1716) "    Fie upon't! foh! About, my brain! Hum, I have heard"
1717) "    That guilty creatures, sitting at a play,"
1718) "    Have by the very cunning of the scene"
1719) "    Been struck so to the soul that presently"
1720) "    They have proclaim'd their malefactions;"
1721) "    For murther, though it have no tongue, will speak"
1722) "    With most miraculous organ, I'll have these Players"
1723) "    Play something like the murther of my father"
1724) "    Before mine uncle. I'll observe his looks;"
1725) "    I'll tent him to the quick. If he but blench,"
1726) "    I know my course. The spirit that I have seen"
1727) "    May be a devil; and the devil hath power"
1728) "    T' assume a pleasing shape; yea, and perhaps"
1729) "    Out of my weakness and my melancholy,"
1730) "    As he is very potent with such spirits,"
1731) "    Abuses me to damn me. I'll have grounds"
1732) "    More relative than this. The play's the thing"
1733) "    Wherein I'll catch the conscience of the King.         Exit."
1734) "ACT III. Scene I."
1735) "Elsinore. A room in the Castle."
1736) "Enter King, Queen, Polonius, Ophelia, Rosencrantz, Guildenstern, and Lords."
1737) "  King. And can you by no drift of circumstance"
1738) "    Get from him why he puts on this confusion,"
1739) "    Grating so harshly all his days of quiet"
1740) "    With turbulent and dangerous lunacy?"
1741) "  Ros. He does confess he feels himself distracted,"
1742) "    But from what cause he will by no means speak."
1743) "  Guil. Nor do we find him forward to be sounded,"
1744) "    But with a crafty madness keeps aloof"
1745) "    When we would bring him on to some confession"
1746) "    Of his true state."
1747) "  Queen. Did he receive you well?"
1748) "  Ros. Most like a gentleman."
1749) "  Guil. But with much forcing of his disposition."
1750) "  Ros. Niggard of question, but of our demands"
1751) "    Most free in his reply."
1752) "  Queen. Did you assay him  "
1753) "    To any pastime?"
1754) "  Ros. Madam, it so fell out that certain players"
1755) "    We o'erraught on the way. Of these we told him,"
1756) "    And there did seem in him a kind of joy"
1757) "    To hear of it. They are here about the court,"
1758) "    And, as I think, they have already order"
1759) "    This night to play before him."
1760) "  Pol. 'Tis most true;"
1761) "    And he beseech'd me to entreat your Majesties"
1762) "    To hear and see the matter."
1763) "  King. With all my heart, and it doth much content me"
1764) "    To hear him so inclin'd."
1765) "    Good gentlemen, give him a further edge"
1766) "    And drive his purpose on to these delights."
1767) "  Ros. We shall, my lord."
1768) "                            Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern."
1769) "  King. Sweet Gertrude, leave us too;"
1770) "    For we have closely sent for Hamlet hither,"
1771) "    That he, as 'twere by accident, may here"
1772) "    Affront Ophelia.  "
1773) "    Her father and myself (lawful espials)"
1774) "    Will so bestow ourselves that, seeing unseen,"
1775) "    We may of their encounter frankly judge"
1776) "    And gather by him, as he is behav'd,"
1777) "    If't be th' affliction of his love, or no,"
1778) "    That thus he suffers for."
1779) "  Queen. I shall obey you;"
1780) "    And for your part, Ophelia, I do wish"
1781) "    That your good beauties be the happy cause"
1782) "    Of Hamlet's wildness. So shall I hope your virtues"
1783) "    Will bring him to his wonted way again,"
1784) "    To both your honours."
1785) "  Oph. Madam, I wish it may."
1786) "                                                   [Exit Queen.]"
1787) "  Pol. Ophelia, walk you here.- Gracious, so please you,"
1788) "    We will bestow ourselves.- [To Ophelia] Read on this book,"
1789) "    That show of such an exercise may colour"
1790) "    Your loneliness.- We are oft to blame in this,"
1791) "    'Tis too much prov'd, that with devotion's visage"
1792) "    And pious action we do sugar o'er  "
1793) "    The Devil himself."
1794) "  King. [aside] O, 'tis too true!"
1795) "    How smart a lash that speech doth give my conscience!"
1796) "    The harlot's cheek, beautied with plast'ring art,"
1797) "    Is not more ugly to the thing that helps it"
1798) "    Than is my deed to my most painted word."
1799) "    O heavy burthen!"
1800) "  Pol. I hear him coming. Let's withdraw, my lord."
1801) "                                      Exeunt King and Polonius]."
1802) "                           Enter Hamlet."
1803) "  Ham. To be, or not to be- that is the question:"
1804) "    Whether 'tis nobler in the mind to suffer"
1805) "    The slings and arrows of outrageous fortune"
1806) "    Or to take arms against a sea of troubles,"
1807) "    And by opposing end them. To die- to sleep-"
1808) "    No more; and by a sleep to say we end"
1809) "    The heartache, and the thousand natural shocks"
1810) "    That flesh is heir to. 'Tis a consummation  "
1811) "    Devoutly to be wish'd. To die- to sleep."
1812) "    To sleep- perchance to dream: ay, there's the rub!"
1813) "    For in that sleep of death what dreams may come"
1814) "    When we have shuffled off this mortal coil,"
1815) "    Must give us pause. There's the respect"
1816) "    That makes calamity of so long life."
1817) "    For who would bear the whips and scorns of time,"
1818) "    Th' oppressor's wrong, the proud man's contumely,"
1819) "    The pangs of despis'd love, the law's delay,"
1820) "    The insolence of office, and the spurns"
1821) "    That patient merit of th' unworthy takes,"
1822) "    When he himself might his quietus make"
1823) "    With a bare bodkin? Who would these fardels bear,"
1824) "    To grunt and sweat under a weary life,"
1825) "    But that the dread of something after death-"
1826) "    The undiscover'd country, from whose bourn"
1827) "    No traveller returns- puzzles the will,"
1828) "    And makes us rather bear those ills we have"
1829) "    Than fly to others that we know not of?"
1830) "    Thus conscience does make cowards of us all,  "
1831) "    And thus the native hue of resolution"
1832) "    Is sicklied o'er with the pale cast of thought,"
1833) "    And enterprises of great pith and moment"
1834) "    With this regard their currents turn awry"
1835) "    And lose the name of action.- Soft you now!"
1836) "    The fair Ophelia!- Nymph, in thy orisons"
1837) "    Be all my sins rememb'red."
1838) "  Oph. Good my lord,"
1839) "    How does your honour for this many a day?"
1840) "  Ham. I humbly thank you; well, well, well."
1841) "  Oph. My lord, I have remembrances of yours"
1842) "    That I have longed long to re-deliver."
1843) "    I pray you, now receive them."
1844) "  Ham. No, not I!"
1845) "    I never gave you aught."
1846) "  Oph. My honour'd lord, you know right well you did,"
1847) "    And with them words of so sweet breath compos'd"
1848) "    As made the things more rich. Their perfume lost,"
1849) "    Take these again; for to the noble mind"
1850) "    Rich gifts wax poor when givers prove unkind.  "
1851) "    There, my lord."
1852) "  Ham. Ha, ha! Are you honest?"
1853) "  Oph. My lord?"
1854) "  Ham. Are you fair?"
1855) "  Oph. What means your lordship?"
1856) "  Ham. That if you be honest and fair, your honesty should admit no"
1857) "    discourse to your beauty."
1858) "  Oph. Could beauty, my lord, have better commerce than with honesty?"
1859) "  Ham. Ay, truly; for the power of beauty will sooner transform"
1860) "    honesty from what it is to a bawd than the force of honesty can"
1861) "    translate beauty into his likeness. This was sometime a paradox,"
1862) "    but now the time gives it proof. I did love you once."
1863) "  Oph. Indeed, my lord, you made me believe so."
1864) "  Ham. You should not have believ'd me; for virtue cannot so"
1865) "    inoculate our old stock but we shall relish of it. I loved you"
1866) "    not."
1867) "  Oph. I was the more deceived."
1868) "  Ham. Get thee to a nunnery! Why wouldst thou be a breeder of"
1869) "    sinners? I am myself indifferent honest, but yet I could accuse"
1870) "    me of such things that it were better my mother had not borne me.  "
1871) "    I am very proud, revengeful, ambitious; with more offences at my"
1872) "    beck than I have thoughts to put them in, imagination to give"
1873) "    them shape, or time to act them in. What should such fellows as I"
1874) "    do, crawling between earth and heaven? We are arrant knaves all;"
1875) "    believe none of us. Go thy ways to a nunnery. Where's your"
1876) "    father?"
1877) "  Oph. At home, my lord."
1878) "  Ham. Let the doors be shut upon him, that he may play the fool"
1879) "    nowhere but in's own house. Farewell."
1880) "  Oph. O, help him, you sweet heavens!"
1881) "  Ham. If thou dost marry, I'll give thee this plague for thy dowry:"
1882) "    be thou as chaste as ice, as pure as snow, thou shalt not escape"
1883) "    calumny. Get thee to a nunnery. Go, farewell. Or if thou wilt"
1884) "    needs marry, marry a fool; for wise men know well enough what"
1885) "    monsters you make of them. To a nunnery, go; and quickly too."
1886) "    Farewell."
1887) "  Oph. O heavenly powers, restore him!"
1888) "  Ham. I have heard of your paintings too, well enough. God hath"
1889) "    given you one face, and you make yourselves another. You jig, you"
1890) "    amble, and you lisp; you nickname God's creatures and make your  "
1891) "    wantonness your ignorance. Go to, I'll no more on't! it hath made"
1892) "    me mad. I say, we will have no moe marriages. Those that are"
1893) "    married already- all but one- shall live; the rest shall keep as"
1894) "    they are. To a nunnery, go.                            Exit."
1895) "  Oph. O, what a noble mind is here o'erthrown!"
1896) "    The courtier's, scholar's, soldier's, eye, tongue, sword,"
1897) "    Th' expectancy and rose of the fair state,"
1898) "    The glass of fashion and the mould of form,"
1899) "    Th' observ'd of all observers- quite, quite down!"
1900) "    And I, of ladies most deject and wretched,"
1901) "    That suck'd the honey of his music vows,"
1902) "    Now see that noble and most sovereign reason,"
1903) "    Like sweet bells jangled, out of tune and harsh;"
1904) "    That unmatch'd form and feature of blown youth"
1905) "    Blasted with ecstasy. O, woe is me"
1906) "    T' have seen what I have seen, see what I see!"
1907) "                   Enter King and Polonius."
1908) "  King. Love? his affections do not that way tend;  "
1909) "    Nor what he spake, though it lack'd form a little,"
1910) "    Was not like madness. There's something in his soul"
1911) "    O'er which his melancholy sits on brood;"
1912) "    And I do doubt the hatch and the disclose"
1913) "    Will be some danger; which for to prevent,"
1914) "    I have in quick determination"
1915) "    Thus set it down: he shall with speed to England"
1916) "    For the demand of our neglected tribute."
1917) "    Haply the seas, and countries different,"
1918) "    With variable objects, shall expel"
1919) "    This something-settled matter in his heart,"
1920) "    Whereon his brains still beating puts him thus"
1921) "    From fashion of himself. What think you on't?"
1922) "  Pol. It shall do well. But yet do I believe"
1923) "    The origin and commencement of his grief"
1924) "    Sprung from neglected love.- How now, Ophelia?"
1925) "    You need not tell us what Lord Hamlet said."
1926) "    We heard it all.- My lord, do as you please;"
1927) "    But if you hold it fit, after the play"
1928) "    Let his queen mother all alone entreat him  "
1929) "    To show his grief. Let her be round with him;"
1930) "    And I'll be plac'd so please you, in the ear"
1931) "    Of all their conference. If she find him not,"
1932) "    To England send him; or confine him where"
1933) "    Your wisdom best shall think."
1934) "  King. It shall be so."
1935) "    Madness in great ones must not unwatch'd go.         Exeunt."
1936) "Scene II."
1937) "Elsinore. hall in the Castle."
1938) "Enter Hamlet and three of the Players."
1939) "  Ham. Speak the speech, I pray you, as I pronounc'd it to you,"
1940) "    trippingly on the tongue. But if you mouth it, as many of our"
1941) "    players do, I had as live the town crier spoke my lines. Nor do"
1942) "    not saw the air too much with your hand, thus, but use all"
1943) "    gently; for in the very torrent, tempest, and (as I may say)"
1944) "    whirlwind of your passion, you must acquire and beget a"
1945) "    temperance that may give it smoothness. O, it offends me to the"
1946) "    soul to hear a robustious periwig-pated fellow tear a passion to"
1947) "    tatters, to very rags, to split the cars of the groundlings, who"
1948) "    (for the most part) are capable of nothing but inexplicable dumb"
1949) "    shows and noise. I would have such a fellow whipp'd for o'erdoing"
1950) "    Termagant. It out-herods Herod. Pray you avoid it."
1951) "  Player. I warrant your honour."
1952) "  Ham. Be not too tame neither; but let your own discretion be your"
1953) "    tutor. Suit the action to the word, the word to the action; with"
1954) "    this special observance, that you o'erstep not the modesty of"
1955) "    nature: for anything so overdone is from the purpose of playing,  "
1956) "    whose end, both at the first and now, was and is, to hold, as"
1957) "    'twere, the mirror up to nature; to show Virtue her own feature,"
1958) "    scorn her own image, and the very age and body of the time his"
1959) "    form and pressure. Now this overdone, or come tardy off, though"
1960) "    it make the unskilful laugh, cannot but make the judicious"
1961) "    grieve; the censure of the which one must in your allowance"
1962) "    o'erweigh a whole theatre of others. O, there be players that I"
1963) "    have seen play, and heard others praise, and that highly (not to"
1964) "    speak it profanely), that, neither having the accent of"
1965) "    Christians, nor the gait of Christian, pagan, nor man, have so"
1966) "    strutted and bellowed that I have thought some of Nature's"
1967) "    journeymen had made men, and not made them well, they imitated"
1968) "    humanity so abominably."
1969) "  Player. I hope we have reform'd that indifferently with us, sir."
1970) "  Ham. O, reform it altogether! And let those that play your clowns"
1971) "    speak no more than is set down for them. For there be of them"
1972) "    that will themselves laugh, to set on some quantity of barren"
1973) "    spectators to laugh too, though in the mean time some necessary"
1974) "    question of the play be then to be considered. That's villanous"
1975) "    and shows a most pitiful ambition in the fool that uses it. Go  "
1976) "    make you ready."

2101) "              Enter [two Players as] King and Queen.  "
2102) "    King. Full thirty times hath Phoebus' cart gone round"
2103) "      Neptune's salt wash and Tellus' orbed ground,"
2104) "      And thirty dozed moons with borrowed sheen"
2105) "      About the world have times twelve thirties been,"

2107) "      Unite comutual in most sacred bands."

2109) "      Make us again count o'er ere love be done!"

2111) "      So far from cheer and from your former state."

2113) "      Discomfort you, my lord, it nothing must;"
2114) "      For women's fear and love holds quantity,"
2115) "      In neither aught, or in extremity."
2116) "      Now what my love is, proof hath made you know;"
2117) "      And as my love is siz'd, my fear is so."
2118) "      Where love is great, the littlest doubts are fear;"
2119) "      Where little fears grow great, great love grows there."

2133) "      When second husband kisses me in bed."

2135) "      But what we do determine oft we break."
2136) "      Purpose is but the slave to memory,"
2137) "      Of violent birth, but poor validity;"
2138) "      Which now, like fruit unripe, sticks on the tree,  "
2139) "      But fill unshaken when they mellow be."
2140) "      Most necessary 'tis that we forget"
2141) "      To pay ourselves what to ourselves is debt."
2142) "      What to ourselves in passion we propose,"
2143) "      The passion ending, doth the purpose lose."
2144) "      The violence of either grief or joy"
2145) "      Their own enactures with themselves destroy."
2146) "      Where joy most revels, grief doth most lament;"
2147) "      Grief joys, joy grieves, on slender accident."
2148) "      This world is not for aye, nor 'tis not strange"
2149) "      That even our loves should with our fortunes change;"
2150) "      For 'tis a question left us yet to prove,"
2151) "      Whether love lead fortune, or else fortune love."
2152) "      The great man down, you mark his favourite flies,"
2153) "      The poor advanc'd makes friends of enemies;"
2154) "      And hitherto doth love on fortune tend,"
2155) "      For who not needs shall never lack a friend,"
2156) "      And who in want a hollow friend doth try,"
2157) "      Directly seasons him his enemy."
2158) "      But, orderly to end where I begun,  "
2159) "      Our wills and fates do so contrary run"
2160) "      That our devices still are overthrown;"
2161) "      Our thoughts are ours, their ends none of our own."
2162) "      So think thou wilt no second husband wed;"
2163) "      But die thy thoughts when thy first lord is dead."

2383) "    O, my offence is rank, it smells to heaven;"

2399) "    Can serve my turn? 'Forgive me my foul murther'?"
2400) "    That cannot be; since I am still possess'd"
2401) "    Of those effects for which I did the murther-"
2402) "    My crown, mine own ambition, and my queen."
2403) "    May one be pardon'd and retain th' offence?"
2404) "    In the corrupted currents of this world"
2405) "    Offence's gilded hand may shove by justice,"
2406) "    And oft 'tis seen the wicked prize itself"
2407) "    Buys out the law; but 'tis not so above."
2408) "    There is no shuffling; there the action lies"
2409) "    In his true nature, and we ourselves compell'd,"
2410) "    Even to the teeth and forehead of our faults,"
2411) "    To give in evidence. What then? What rests?"
2412) "    Try what repentance can. What can it not?"
2413) "    Yet what can it when one cannot repent?"
2414) "    O wretched state! O bosom black as death!"
2415) "    O limed soul, that, struggling to be free,"
2416) "    Art more engag'd! Help, angels! Make assay.  "
2417) "    Bow, stubborn knees; and heart with strings of steel,"
2418) "    Be soft as sinews of the new-born babe!"
2419) "    All may be well.                                  He kneels."
2420) "                         Enter Hamlet."
2421) "  Ham. Now might I do it pat, now he is praying;"
2422) "    And now I'll do't. And so he goes to heaven,"
2423) "    And so am I reveng'd. That would be scann'd."
2424) "    A villain kills my father; and for that,"
2425) "    I, his sole son, do this same villain send"
2426) "    To heaven."
2427) "    Why, this is hire and salary, not revenge!"
2428) "    He took my father grossly, full of bread,"
2429) "    With all his crimes broad blown, as flush as May;"
2430) "    And how his audit stands, who knows save heaven?"
2431) "    But in our circumstance and course of thought,"
2432) "    'Tis heavy with him; and am I then reveng'd,"
2433) "    To take him in the purging of his soul,"
2434) "    When he is fit and seasoned for his passage?  "
2435) "    No."
2436) "    Up, sword, and know thou a more horrid hent."
2437) "    When he is drunk asleep; or in his rage;"
2438) "    Or in th' incestuous pleasure of his bed;"
2439) "    At gaming, swearing, or about some act"
2440) "    That has no relish of salvation in't-"
2441) "    Then trip him, that his heels may kick at heaven,"
2442) "    And that his soul may be as damn'd and black"
2443) "    As hell, whereto it goes. My mother stays."
2444) "    This physic but prolongs thy sickly days.              Exit."
2445) "  King. [rises] My words fly up, my thoughts remain below."
2446) "    Words without thoughts never to heaven go.             Exit."
2447) "Scene IV."
2448) "The Queen's closet."
2449) "Enter Queen and Polonius."
2450) "  Pol. He will come straight. Look you lay home to him."
2451) "    Tell him his pranks have been too broad to bear with,"
2452) "    And that your Grace hath screen'd and stood between"
2453) "    Much heat and him. I'll silence me even here."
2454) "    Pray you be round with him."
2455) "  Ham. (within) Mother, mother, mother!"
2456) "  Queen. I'll warrant you; fear me not. Withdraw; I hear him coming."
2457) "                              [Polonius hides behind the arras.]"
2458) "                          Enter Hamlet."
2459) "  Ham. Now, mother, what's the matter?"
2460) "  Queen. Hamlet, thou hast thy father much offended."
2461) "  Ham. Mother, you have my father much offended."
2462) "  Queen. Come, come, you answer with an idle tongue."
2463) "  Ham. Go, go, you question with a wicked tongue."
2464) "  Queen. Why, how now, Hamlet?  "
2465) "  Ham. What's the matter now?"
2466) "  Queen. Have you forgot me?"
2467) "  Ham. No, by the rood, not so!"
2468) "    You are the Queen, your husband's brother's wife,"
2469) "    And (would it were not so!) you are my mother."
2470) "  Queen. Nay, then I'll set those to you that can speak."
2471) "  Ham. Come, come, and sit you down. You shall not budge I"
2472) "    You go not till I set you up a glass"
2473) "    Where you may see the inmost part of you."
2474) "  Queen. What wilt thou do? Thou wilt not murther me?"
2475) "    Help, help, ho!"
2476) "  Pol. [behind] What, ho! help, help, help!"
2477) "  Ham. [draws] How now? a rat? Dead for a ducat, dead!"
2478) "            [Makes a pass through the arras and] kills Polonius."
2479) "  Pol. [behind] O, I am slain!"
2480) "  Queen. O me, what hast thou done?"
2481) "  Ham. Nay, I know not. Is it the King?"
2482) "  Queen. O, what a rash and bloody deed is this!"
2483) "  Ham. A bloody deed- almost as bad, good mother,"
2484) "    As kill a king, and marry with his brother.  "
2485) "  Queen. As kill a king?"
2486) "  Ham. Ay, lady, it was my word."
2487) "                         [Lifts up the arras and sees Polonius.]"
2488) "    Thou wretched, rash, intruding fool, farewell!"
2489) "    I took thee for thy better. Take thy fortune."
2490) "    Thou find'st to be too busy is some danger."
2491) "    Leave wringing of your hinds. Peace! sit you down"
2492) "    And let me wring your heart; for so I shall"
2493) "    If it be made of penetrable stuff;"
2494) "    If damned custom have not braz'd it so"
2495) "    That it is proof and bulwark against sense."
2496) "  Queen. What have I done that thou dar'st wag thy tongue"
2497) "    In noise so rude against me?"
2498) "  Ham. Such an act"
2499) "    That blurs the grace and blush of modesty;"
2500) "    Calls virtue hypocrite; takes off the rose"
2501) "    From the fair forehead of an innocent love,"
2502) "    And sets a blister there; makes marriage vows"
2503) "    As false as dicers' oaths. O, such a deed"
2504) "    As from the body of contraction plucks  "
2505) "    The very soul, and sweet religion makes"
2506) "    A rhapsody of words! Heaven's face doth glow;"
2507) "    Yea, this solidity and compound mass,"
2508) "    With tristful visage, as against the doom,"
2509) "    Is thought-sick at the act."
2510) "  Queen. Ay me, what act,"
2511) "    That roars so loud and thunders in the index?"
2512) "  Ham. Look here upon th's picture, and on this,"
2513) "    The counterfeit presentment of two brothers."
2514) "    See what a grace was seated on this brow;"
2515) "    Hyperion's curls; the front of Jove himself;"
2516) "    An eye like Mars, to threaten and command;"
2517) "    A station like the herald Mercury"
2518) "    New lighted on a heaven-kissing hill:"
2519) "    A combination and a form indeed"
2520) "    Where every god did seem to set his seal"
2521) "    To give the world assurance of a man."
2522) "    This was your husband. Look you now what follows."
2523) "    Here is your husband, like a mildew'd ear"
2524) "    Blasting his wholesome brother. Have you eyes?  "
2525) "    Could you on this fair mountain leave to feed,"
2526) "    And batten on this moor? Ha! have you eyes"
2527) "    You cannot call it love; for at your age"
2528) "    The heyday in the blood is tame, it's humble,"
2529) "    And waits upon the judgment; and what judgment"
2530) "    Would step from this to this? Sense sure you have,"
2531) "    Else could you not have motion; but sure that sense"
2532) "    Is apoplex'd; for madness would not err,"
2533) "    Nor sense to ecstacy was ne'er so thrall'd"
2534) "    But it reserv'd some quantity of choice"
2535) "    To serve in such a difference. What devil was't"
2536) "    That thus hath cozen'd you at hoodman-blind?"
2537) "    Eyes without feeling, feeling without sight,"
2538) "    Ears without hands or eyes, smelling sans all,"
2539) "    Or but a sickly part of one true sense"
2540) "    Could not so mope."
2541) "    O shame! where is thy blush? Rebellious hell,"
2542) "    If thou canst mutine in a matron's bones,"
2543) "    To flaming youth let virtue be as wax"
2544) "    And melt in her own fire. Proclaim no shame  "
2545) "    When the compulsive ardour gives the charge,"
2546) "    Since frost itself as actively doth burn,"
2547) "    And reason panders will."
2548) "  Queen. O Hamlet, speak no more!"
2549) "    Thou turn'st mine eyes into my very soul,"
2550) "    And there I see such black and grained spots"
2551) "    As will not leave their tinct."
2552) "  Ham. Nay, but to live"
2553) "    In the rank sweat of an enseamed bed,"
2554) "    Stew'd in corruption, honeying and making love"
2555) "    Over the nasty sty!"
2556) "  Queen. O, speak to me no more!"
2557) "    These words like daggers enter in mine ears."
2558) "    No more, sweet Hamlet!"
2559) "  Ham. A murtherer and a villain!"
2560) "    A slave that is not twentieth part the tithe"
2561) "    Of your precedent lord; a vice of kings;"
2562) "    A cutpurse of the empire and the rule,"
2563) "    That from a shelf the precious diadem stole"
2564) "    And put it in his pocket!  "
2565) "  Queen. No more!"
2566) "                Enter the Ghost in his nightgown."
2567) "  Ham. A king of shreds and patches!-"
2568) "    Save me and hover o'er me with your wings,"
2569) "    You heavenly guards! What would your gracious figure?"
2570) "  Queen. Alas, he's mad!"
2571) "  Ham. Do you not come your tardy son to chide,"
2572) "    That, laps'd in time and passion, lets go by"
2573) "    Th' important acting of your dread command?"
2574) "    O, say!"
2575) "  Ghost. Do not forget. This visitation"
2576) "    Is but to whet thy almost blunted purpose."
2577) "    But look, amazement on thy mother sits."
2578) "    O, step between her and her fighting soul"
2579) "    Conceit in weakest bodies strongest works."
2580) "    Speak to her, Hamlet."
2581) "  Ham. How is it with you, lady?"
2582) "  Queen. Alas, how is't with you,  "
2583) "    That you do bend your eye on vacancy,"
2584) "    And with th' encorporal air do hold discourse?"
2585) "    Forth at your eyes your spirits wildly peep;"
2586) "    And, as the sleeping soldiers in th' alarm,"
2587) "    Your bedded hairs, like life in excrements,"
2588) "    Start up and stand an end. O gentle son,"
2589) "    Upon the beat and flame of thy distemper"
2590) "    Sprinkle cool patience! Whereon do you look?"
2591) "  Ham. On him, on him! Look you how pale he glares!"
2592) "    His form and cause conjoin'd, preaching to stones,"
2593) "    Would make them capable.- Do not look upon me,"
2594) "    Lest with this piteous action you convert"
2595) "    My stern effects. Then what I have to do"
2596) "    Will want true colour- tears perchance for blood."
2597) "  Queen. To whom do you speak this?"
2598) "  Ham. Do you see nothing there?"
2599) "  Queen. Nothing at all; yet all that is I see."
2600) "  Ham. Nor did you nothing hear?"
2601) "  Queen. No, nothing but ourselves."
2602) "  Ham. Why, look you there! Look how it steals away!  "
2603) "    My father, in his habit as he liv'd!"
2604) "    Look where he goes even now out at the portal!"
2605) "                                                     Exit Ghost."
2606) "  Queen. This is the very coinage of your brain."
2607) "    This bodiless creation ecstasy"
2608) "    Is very cunning in."
2609) "  Ham. Ecstasy?"
2610) "    My pulse as yours doth temperately keep time"
2611) "    And makes as healthful music. It is not madness"
2612) "    That I have utt'red. Bring me to the test,"
2613) "    And I the matter will reword; which madness"
2614) "    Would gambol from. Mother, for love of grace,"
2615) "    Lay not that flattering unction to your soul"
2616) "    That not your trespass but my madness speaks."
2617) "    It will but skin and film the ulcerous place,"
2618) "    Whiles rank corruption, mining all within,"
2619) "    Infects unseen. Confess yourself to heaven;"
2620) "    Repent what's past; avoid what is to come;"
2621) "    And do not spread the compost on the weeds"
2622) "    To make them ranker. Forgive me this my virtue;  "
2623) "    For in the fatness of these pursy times"
2624) "    Virtue itself of vice must pardon beg-"
2625) "    Yea, curb and woo for leave to do him good."
2626) "  Queen. O Hamlet, thou hast cleft my heart in twain."
2627) "  Ham. O, throw away the worser part of it,"
2628) "    And live the purer with the other half,"
2629) "    Good night- but go not to my uncle's bed."
2630) "    Assume a virtue, if you have it not."
2631) "    That monster, custom, who all sense doth eat"
2632) "    Of habits evil, is angel yet in this,"
2633) "    That to the use of actions fair and good"
2634) "    He likewise gives a frock or livery,"
2635) "    That aptly is put on. Refrain to-night,"
2636) "    And that shall lend a kind of easiness"
2637) "    To the next abstinence; the next more easy;"
2638) "    For use almost can change the stamp of nature,"
2639) "    And either [master] the devil, or throw him out"
2640) "    With wondrous potency. Once more, good night;"
2641) "    And when you are desirous to be blest,"
2642) "    I'll blessing beg of you.- For this same lord,  "
2643) "    I do repent; but heaven hath pleas'd it so,"
2644) "    To punish me with this, and this with me,"
2645) "    That I must be their scourge and minister."
2646) "    I will bestow him, and will answer well"
2647) "    The death I gave him. So again, good night."
2648) "    I must be cruel, only to be kind;"
2649) "    Thus bad begins, and worse remains behind."
2650) "    One word more, good lady."
2651) "  Queen. What shall I do?"
2652) "  Ham. Not this, by no means, that I bid you do:"
2653) "    Let the bloat King tempt you again to bed;"
2654) "    Pinch wanton on your cheek; call you his mouse;"
2655) "    And let him, for a pair of reechy kisses,"
2656) "    Or paddling in your neck with his damn'd fingers,"
2657) "    Make you to ravel all this matter out,"
2658) "    That I essentially am not in madness,"
2659) "    But mad in craft. 'Twere good you let him know;"
2660) "    For who that's but a queen, fair, sober, wise,"
2661) "    Would from a paddock, from a bat, a gib"
2662) "    Such dear concernings hide? Who would do so?  "
2663) "    No, in despite of sense and secrecy,"
2664) "    Unpeg the basket on the house's top,"
2665) "    Let the birds fly, and like the famous ape,"
2666) "    To try conclusions, in the basket creep"
2667) "    And break your own neck down."
2668) "  Queen. Be thou assur'd, if words be made of breath,"
2669) "    And breath of life, I have no life to breathe"
2670) "    What thou hast said to me."
2671) "  Ham. I must to England; you know that?"
2672) "  Queen. Alack,"
2673) "    I had forgot! 'Tis so concluded on."
2674) "  Ham. There's letters seal'd; and my two schoolfellows,"
2675) "    Whom I will trust as I will adders fang'd,"
2676) "    They bear the mandate; they must sweep my way"
2677) "    And marshal me to knavery. Let it work;"
2678) "    For 'tis the sport to have the enginer"
2679) "    Hoist with his own petar; and 't shall go hard"
2680) "    But I will delve one yard below their mines"
2681) "    And blow them at the moon. O, 'tis most sweet"
2682) "    When in one line two crafts directly meet.  "
2683) "    This man shall set me packing."
2684) "    I'll lug the guts into the neighbour room.-"
2685) "    Mother, good night.- Indeed, this counsellor"
2686) "    Is now most still, most secret, and most grave,"
2687) "    Who was in life a foolish peating knave."
2688) "    Come, sir, to draw toward an end with you."
2689) "    Good night, mother."
2690) "                  [Exit the Queen. Then] Exit Hamlet, tugging in"
2691) "                                                       Polonius."
2692) "ACT IV. Scene I."
2693) "Elsinore. A room in the Castle."
2694) "Enter King and Queen, with Rosencrantz and Guildenstern."
2695) "  King. There's matter in these sighs. These profound heaves"
2696) "    You must translate; 'tis fit we understand them."
2697) "    Where is your son?"
2698) "  Queen. Bestow this place on us a little while."
2699) "                          [Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.]"
2700) "    Ah, mine own lord, what have I seen to-night!"
2701) "  King. What, Gertrude? How does Hamlet?"
2702) "  Queen. Mad as the sea and wind when both contend"
2703) "    Which is the mightier. In his lawless fit"
2704) "    Behind the arras hearing something stir,"
2705) "    Whips out his rapier, cries 'A rat, a rat!'"
2706) "    And in this brainish apprehension kills"
2707) "    The unseen good old man."
2708) "  King. O heavy deed!"
2709) "    It had been so with us, had we been there."
2710) "    His liberty is full of threats to all-"
2711) "    To you yourself, to us, to every one.  "
2712) "    Alas, how shall this bloody deed be answer'd?"
2713) "    It will be laid to us, whose providence"
2714) "    Should have kept short, restrain'd, and out of haunt"
2715) "    This mad young man. But so much was our love"
2716) "    We would not understand what was most fit,"
2717) "    But, like the owner of a foul disease,"
2718) "    To keep it from divulging, let it feed"
2719) "    Even on the pith of life. Where is he gone?"
2720) "  Queen. To draw apart the body he hath kill'd;"
2721) "    O'er whom his very madness, like some ore"
2722) "    Among a mineral of metals base,"
2723) "    Shows itself pure. He weeps for what is done."
2724) "  King. O Gertrude, come away!"
2725) "    The sun no sooner shall the mountains touch"
2726) "    But we will ship him hence; and this vile deed"
2727) "    We must with all our majesty and skill"
2728) "    Both countenance and excuse. Ho, Guildenstern!"
2729) "             Enter Rosencrantz and Guildenstern."
2730) "  "
2731) "    Friends both, go join you with some further aid."
2732) "    Hamlet in madness hath Polonius slain,"
2733) "    And from his mother's closet hath he dragg'd him."
2734) "    Go seek him out; speak fair, and bring the body"
2735) "    Into the chapel. I pray you haste in this."
2736) "                          Exeunt [Rosencrantz and Guildenstern]."
2737) "    Come, Gertrude, we'll call up our wisest friends"
2738) "    And let them know both what we mean to do"
2739) "    And what's untimely done. [So haply slander-]"
2740) "    Whose whisper o'er the world's diameter,"
2741) "    As level as the cannon to his blank,"
2742) "    Transports his poisoned shot- may miss our name"
2743) "    And hit the woundless air.- O, come away!"
2744) "    My soul is full of discord and dismay."
2745) "                                                         Exeunt."
2746) "Scene II."
2747) "Elsinore. A passage in the Castle."
2748) "Enter Hamlet."
2749) "  Ham. Safely stow'd."
2750) "  Gentlemen. (within) Hamlet! Lord Hamlet!"
2751) "  Ham. But soft! What noise? Who calls on Hamlet? O, here they come."
2752) "               Enter Rosencrantz and Guildenstern."
2753) "  Ros. What have you done, my lord, with the dead body?"
2754) "  Ham. Compounded it with dust, whereto 'tis kin."
2755) "  Ros. Tell us where 'tis, that we may take it thence"
2756) "    And bear it to the chapel."
2757) "  Ham. Do not believe it."
2758) "  Ros. Believe what?"
2759) "  Ham. That I can keep your counsel, and not mine own. Besides, to be"
2760) "    demanded of a sponge, what replication should be made by the son"
2761) "    of a king?"
2762) "  Ros. Take you me for a sponge, my lord?"
2763) "  Ham. Ay, sir; that soaks up the King's countenance, his rewards,  "
2764) "    his authorities. But such officers do the King best service in"
2765) "    the end. He keeps them, like an ape, in the corner of his jaw;"
2766) "    first mouth'd, to be last Swallowed. When he needs what you have"
2767) "    glean'd, it is but squeezing you and, sponge, you shall be dry"
2768) "    again."
2769) "  Ros. I understand you not, my lord."
2770) "  Ham. I am glad of it. A knavish speech sleeps in a foolish ear."
2771) "  Ros. My lord, you must tell us where the body is and go with us to"
2772) "    the King."
2773) "  Ham. The body is with the King, but the King is not with the body."
2774) "    The King is a thing-"
2775) "  Guil. A thing, my lord?"
2776) "  Ham. Of nothing. Bring me to him. Hide fox, and all after."
2777) "                                                         Exeunt."
2778) "Scene III."
2779) "Elsinore. A room in the Castle."
2780) "Enter King."
2781) "  King. I have sent to seek him and to find the body."
2782) "    How dangerous is it that this man goes loose!"
2783) "    Yet must not we put the strong law on him."
2784) "    He's lov'd of the distracted multitude,"
2785) "    Who like not in their judgment, but their eyes;"
2786) "    And where 'tis so, th' offender's scourge is weigh'd,"
2787) "    But never the offence. To bear all smooth and even,"
2788) "    This sudden sending him away must seem"
2789) "    Deliberate pause. Diseases desperate grown"
2790) "    By desperate appliance are reliev'd,"
2791) "    Or not at all."
2792) "                    Enter Rosencrantz."
2793) "    How now O What hath befall'n?"
2794) "  Ros. Where the dead body is bestow'd, my lord,"
2795) "    We cannot get from him.  "
2796) "  King. But where is he?"
2797) "  Ros. Without, my lord; guarded, to know your pleasure."
2798) "  King. Bring him before us."
2799) "  Ros. Ho, Guildenstern! Bring in my lord."
2800) "        Enter Hamlet and Guildenstern [with Attendants]."
2801) "  King. Now, Hamlet, where's Polonius?"
2802) "  Ham. At supper."
2803) "  King. At supper? Where?"
2804) "  Ham. Not where he eats, but where he is eaten. A certain"
2805) "    convocation of politic worms are e'en at him. Your worm is your"
2806) "    only emperor for diet. We fat all creatures else to fat us, and"
2807) "    we fat ourselves for maggots. Your fat king and your lean beggar"
2808) "    is but variable service- two dishes, but to one table. That's the"
2809) "    end."
2810) "  King. Alas, alas!"
2811) "  Ham. A man may fish with the worm that hath eat of a king, and eat"
2812) "    of the fish that hath fed of that worm."
2813) "  King. What dost thou mean by this?  "
2814) "  Ham. Nothing but to show you how a king may go a progress through"
2815) "    the guts of a beggar."
2816) "  King. Where is Polonius?"
2817) "  Ham. In heaven. Send thither to see. If your messenger find him not"
2818) "    there, seek him i' th' other place yourself. But indeed, if you"
2819) "    find him not within this month, you shall nose him as you go up"
2820) "    the stair, into the lobby."
2821) "  King. Go seek him there. [To Attendants.]"
2822) "  Ham. He will stay till you come."
2823) "                                            [Exeunt Attendants.]"
2824) "  King. Hamlet, this deed, for thine especial safety,-"
2825) "    Which we do tender as we dearly grieve"
2826) "    For that which thou hast done,- must send thee hence"
2827) "    With fiery quickness. Therefore prepare thyself."
2828) "    The bark is ready and the wind at help,"
2829) "    Th' associates tend, and everything is bent"
2830) "    For England."
2831) "  Ham. For England?"
2832) "  King. Ay, Hamlet."
2833) "  Ham. Good.  "
2834) "  King. So is it, if thou knew'st our purposes."
2835) "  Ham. I see a cherub that sees them. But come, for England!"
2836) "    Farewell, dear mother."
2837) "  King. Thy loving father, Hamlet."
2838) "  Ham. My mother! Father and mother is man and wife; man and wife is"
2839) "    one flesh; and so, my mother. Come, for England!"
2840) "Exit."
2841) "  King. Follow him at foot; tempt him with speed aboard."
2842) "    Delay it not; I'll have him hence to-night."
2843) "    Away! for everything is seal'd and done"
2844) "    That else leans on th' affair. Pray you make haste."
2845) "                            Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern]"
2846) "    And, England, if my love thou hold'st at aught,-"
2847) "    As my great power thereof may give thee sense,"
2848) "    Since yet thy cicatrice looks raw and red"
2849) "    After the Danish sword, and thy free awe"
2850) "    Pays homage to us,- thou mayst not coldly set"
2851) "    Our sovereign process, which imports at full,"
2852) "    By letters congruing to that effect,"
2853) "    The present death of Hamlet. Do it, England;  "
2854) "    For like the hectic in my blood he rages,"
2855) "    And thou must cure me. Till I know 'tis done,"
2856) "    Howe'er my haps, my joys were ne'er begun.             Exit."
2857) "Scene IV."
2858) "Near Elsinore."
2859) "Enter Fortinbras with his Army over the stage."
2860) "  For. Go, Captain, from me greet the Danish king."
2861) "    Tell him that by his license Fortinbras"
2862) "    Craves the conveyance of a promis'd march"
2863) "    Over his kingdom. You know the rendezvous."
2864) "    if that his Majesty would aught with us,"
2865) "    We shall express our duty in his eye;"
2866) "    And let him know so."
2867) "  Capt. I will do't, my lord."
2868) "  For. Go softly on."
2869) "                                   Exeunt [all but the Captain]."
2870) "       Enter Hamlet, Rosencrantz, [Guildenstern,] and others."
2871) "  Ham. Good sir, whose powers are these?"
2872) "  Capt. They are of Norway, sir."
2873) "  Ham. How purpos'd, sir, I pray you?"
2874) "  Capt. Against some part of Poland.  "
2875) "  Ham. Who commands them, sir?"
2876) "  Capt. The nephew to old Norway, Fortinbras."
2877) "  Ham. Goes it against the main of Poland, sir,"
2878) "    Or for some frontier?"
2879) "  Capt. Truly to speak, and with no addition,"
2880) "    We go to gain a little patch of ground"
2881) "    That hath in it no profit but the name."
2882) "    To pay five ducats, five, I would not farm it;"
2883) "    Nor will it yield to Norway or the Pole"
2884) "    A ranker rate, should it be sold in fee."
2885) "  Ham. Why, then the Polack never will defend it."
2886) "  Capt. Yes, it is already garrison'd."
2887) "  Ham. Two thousand souls and twenty thousand ducats"
2888) "    Will not debate the question of this straw."
2889) "    This is th' imposthume of much wealth and peace,"
2890) "    That inward breaks, and shows no cause without"
2891) "    Why the man dies.- I humbly thank you, sir."
2892) "  Capt. God b' wi' you, sir.                             [Exit.]"
2893) "  Ros. Will't please you go, my lord?"
2894) "  Ham. I'll be with you straight. Go a little before.  "
2895) "                                        [Exeunt all but Hamlet.]"
2896) "    How all occasions do inform against me"
2897) "    And spur my dull revenge! What is a man,"
2898) "    If his chief good and market of his time"
2899) "    Be but to sleep and feed? A beast, no more."
2900) "    Sure he that made us with such large discourse,"
2901) "    Looking before and after, gave us not"
2902) "    That capability and godlike reason"
2903) "    To fust in us unus'd. Now, whether it be"
2904) "    Bestial oblivion, or some craven scruple"
2905) "    Of thinking too precisely on th' event,-"
2906) "    A thought which, quarter'd, hath but one part wisdom"
2907) "    And ever three parts coward,- I do not know"
2908) "    Why yet I live to say 'This thing's to do,'"
2909) "    Sith I have cause, and will, and strength, and means"
2910) "    To do't. Examples gross as earth exhort me."
2911) "    Witness this army of such mass and charge,"
2912) "    Led by a delicate and tender prince,"
2913) "    Whose spirit, with divine ambition puff'd,"
2914) "    Makes mouths at the invisible event,  "
2915) "    Exposing what is mortal and unsure"
2916) "    To all that fortune, death, and danger dare,"
2917) "    Even for an eggshell. Rightly to be great"
2918) "    Is not to stir without great argument,"
2919) "    But greatly to find quarrel in a straw"
2920) "    When honour's at the stake. How stand I then,"
2921) "    That have a father klll'd, a mother stain'd,"
2922) "    Excitements of my reason and my blood,"
2923) "    And let all sleep, while to my shame I see"
2924) "    The imminent death of twenty thousand men"
2925) "    That for a fantasy and trick of fame"
2926) "    Go to their graves like beds, fight for a plot"
2927) "    Whereon the numbers cannot try the cause,"
2928) "    Which is not tomb enough and continent"
2929) "    To hide the slain? O, from this time forth,"
2930) "    My thoughts be bloody, or be nothing worth!            Exit."

3305) "    I have seen myself, and serv'd against, the French,"
3306) "    And they can well on horseback; but this gallant  "
3307) "    Had witchcraft in't. He grew unto his seat,"
3308) "    And to such wondrous doing brought his horse"
3309) "    As had he been incorps'd and demi-natur'd"
3310) "    With the brave beast. So far he topp'd my thought"
3311) "    That I, in forgery of shapes and tricks,"
3312) "    Come short of what he did."
3313) "  Laer. A Norman was't?"
3314) "  King. A Norman."
3315) "  Laer. Upon my life, Lamound."
3316) "  King. The very same."
3317) "  Laer. I know him well. He is the broach indeed"
3318) "    And gem of all the nation."
3319) "  King. He made confession of you;"
3320) "    And gave you such a masterly report"
3321) "    For art and exercise in your defence,"
3322) "    And for your rapier most especially,"
3323) "    That he cried out 'twould be a sight indeed"
3324) "    If one could match you. The scrimers of their nation"
3325) "    He swore had neither motion, guard, nor eye,"
3326) "    If you oppos'd them. Sir, this report of his  "
3327) "    Did Hamlet so envenom with his envy"
3328) "    That he could nothing do but wish and beg"
3329) "    Your sudden coming o'er to play with you."
3330) "    Now, out of this-"
3331) "  Laer. What out of this, my lord?"
3332) "  King. Laertes, was your father dear to you?"
3333) "    Or are you like the painting of a sorrow,"
3334) "    A face without a heart,'"
3335) "  Laer. Why ask you this?"
3336) "  King. Not that I think you did not love your father;"
3337) "    But that I know love is begun by time,"
3338) "    And that I see, in passages of proof,"
3339) "    Time qualifies the spark and fire of it."
3340) "    There lives within the very flame of love"
3341) "    A kind of wick or snuff that will abate it;"
3342) "    And nothing is at a like goodness still;"
3343) "    For goodness, growing to a plurisy,"
3344) "    Dies in his own too-much. That we would do,"
3345) "    We should do when we would; for this 'would' changes,"
3346) "    And hath abatements and delays as many  "
3347) "    As there are tongues, are hands, are accidents;"
3348) "    And then this 'should' is like a spendthrift sigh,"
3349) "    That hurts by easing. But to the quick o' th' ulcer!"
3350) "    Hamlet comes back. What would you undertake"
3351) "    To show yourself your father's son in deed"
3352) "    More than in words?"
3353) "  Laer. To cut his throat i' th' church!"
3354) "  King. No place indeed should murther sanctuarize;"
3355) "    Revenge should have no bounds. But, good Laertes,"
3356) "    Will you do this? Keep close within your chamber."
3357) "    Will return'd shall know you are come home."
3358) "    We'll put on those shall praise your excellence"
3359) "    And set a double varnish on the fame"
3360) "    The Frenchman gave you; bring you in fine together"
3361) "    And wager on your heads. He, being remiss,"
3362) "    Most generous, and free from all contriving,"
3363) "    Will not peruse the foils; so that with ease,"
3364) "    Or with a little shuffling, you may choose"
3365) "    A sword unbated, and, in a pass of practice,"
3366) "    Requite him for your father.  "
3367) "  Laer. I will do't!"
3368) "    And for that purpose I'll anoint my sword."
3369) "    I bought an unction of a mountebank,"
3370) "    So mortal that, but dip a knife in it,"
3371) "    Where it draws blood no cataplasm so rare,"
3372) "    Collected from all simples that have virtue"
3373) "    Under the moon, can save the thing from death"
3374) "    This is but scratch'd withal. I'll touch my point"
3375) "    With this contagion, that, if I gall him slightly,"
3376) "    It may be death."
3377) "  King. Let's further think of this,"
3378) "    Weigh what convenience both of time and means"
3379) "    May fit us to our shape. If this should fall,"
3380) "    And that our drift look through our bad performance."
3381) "    'Twere better not assay'd. Therefore this project"
3382) "    Should have a back or second, that might hold"
3383) "    If this did blast in proof. Soft! let me see."
3384) "    We'll make a solemn wager on your cunnings-"
3385) "    I ha't!"
3386) "    When in your motion you are hot and dry-  "
3387) "    As make your bouts more violent to that end-"
3388) "    And that he calls for drink, I'll have prepar'd him"
3389) "    A chalice for the nonce; whereon but sipping,"
3390) "    If he by chance escape your venom'd stuck,"
3391) "    Our purpose may hold there.- But stay, what noise,"
3392) "                           Enter Queen."
3393) "    How now, sweet queen?"
3394) "  Queen. One woe doth tread upon another's heel,"
3395) "    So fast they follow. Your sister's drown'd, Laertes."
3396) "  Laer. Drown'd! O, where?"
3397) "  Queen. There is a willow grows aslant a brook,"
3398) "    That shows his hoar leaves in the glassy stream."
3399) "    There with fantastic garlands did she come"
3400) "    Of crowflowers, nettles, daisies, and long purples,"
3401) "    That liberal shepherds give a grosser name,"
3402) "    But our cold maids do dead men's fingers call them."
3403) "    There on the pendant boughs her coronet weeds"
3404) "    Clamb'ring to hang, an envious sliver broke,  "
3405) "    When down her weedy trophies and herself"
3406) "    Fell in the weeping brook. Her clothes spread wide"
3407) "    And, mermaid-like, awhile they bore her up;"
3408) "    Which time she chaunted snatches of old tunes,"
3409) "    As one incapable of her own distress,"
3410) "    Or like a creature native and indued"
3411) "    Unto that element; but long it could not be"
3412) "    Till that her garments, heavy with their drink,"
3413) "    Pull'd the poor wretch from her melodious lay"
3414) "    To muddy death."
3415) "  Laer. Alas, then she is drown'd?"
3416) "  Queen. Drown'd, drown'd."
3417) "  Laer. Too much of water hast thou, poor Ophelia,"
3418) "    And therefore I forbid my tears; but yet"
3419) "    It is our trick; nature her custom holds,"
3420) "    Let shame say what it will. When these are gone,"
3421) "    The woman will be out. Adieu, my lord."
3422) "    I have a speech of fire, that fain would blaze"
3423) "    But that this folly douts it.                          Exit."
3424) "  King. Let's follow, Gertrude.  "

3427) "    Therefore let's follow."

3429) "ACT V. Scene I."
3430) "Elsinore. A churchyard."
3431) "Enter two Clowns, [with spades and pickaxes]."
3432) "  Clown. Is she to be buried in Christian burial when she wilfully"
3433) "    seeks her own salvation?"
3434) "  Other. I tell thee she is; therefore make her grave straight."
3435) "    The crowner hath sate on her, and finds it Christian burial."
3436) "  Clown. How can that be, unless she drown'd herself in her own"
3437) "    defence?"
3438) "  Other. Why, 'tis found so."
3439) "  Clown. It must be se offendendo; it cannot be else. For here lies"
3440) "    the point: if I drown myself wittingly, it argues an act; and an"
3441) "    act hath three branches-it is to act, to do, and to perform;"
3442) "    argal, she drown'd herself wittingly."
3443) "  Other. Nay, but hear you, Goodman Delver!"
3444) "  Clown. Give me leave. Here lies the water; good. Here stands the"
3445) "    man; good. If the man go to this water and drown himself, it is,"
3446) "    will he nill he, he goes- mark you that. But if the water come to"
3447) "    him and drown him, he drowns not himself. Argal, he that is not"
3448) "    guilty of his own death shortens not his own life.  "
3449) "  Other. But is this law?"
3450) "  Clown. Ay, marry, is't- crowner's quest law."
3451) "  Other. Will you ha' the truth an't? If this had not been a"
3452) "    gentlewoman, she should have been buried out o' Christian burial."
3453) "  Clown. Why, there thou say'st! And the more pity that great folk"
3454) "    should have count'nance in this world to drown or hang themselves"
3455) "    more than their even-Christen. Come, my spade! There is no"
3456) "    ancient gentlemen but gard'ners, ditchers, and grave-makers. They"
3457) "    hold up Adam's profession."
3458) "  Other. Was he a gentleman?"
3459) "  Clown. 'A was the first that ever bore arms."
3460) "  Other. Why, he had none."
3461) "  Clown. What, art a heathen? How dost thou understand the Scripture?"
3462) "    The Scripture says Adam digg'd. Could he dig without arms? I'll"
3463) "    put another question to thee. If thou answerest me not to the"
3464) "    purpose, confess thyself-"
3465) "  Other. Go to!"
3466) "  Clown. What is he that builds stronger than either the mason, the"
3467) "    shipwright, or the carpenter?"
3468) "  Other. The gallows-maker; for that frame outlives a thousand  "
3469) "    tenants."
3470) "  Clown. I like thy wit well, in good faith. The gallows does well."
3471) "    But how does it well? It does well to those that do ill. Now,"
3472) "    thou dost ill to say the gallows is built stronger than the"
3473) "    church. Argal, the gallows may do well to thee. To't again, come!"
3474) "  Other. Who builds stronger than a mason, a shipwright, or a"
3475) "    carpenter?"
3476) "  Clown. Ay, tell me that, and unyoke."
3477) "  Other. Marry, now I can tell!"
3478) "  Clown. To't."
3479) "  Other. Mass, I cannot tell."

3482) "    not mend his pace with beating; and when you are ask'd this"
3483) "    question next, say 'a grave-maker.' The houses he makes lasts"

3488) "       In youth when I did love, did love,"
3489) "         Methought it was very sweet;"
3490) "       To contract- O- the time for- a- my behove,"
3491) "         O, methought there- a- was nothing- a- meet."

3498) "         But age with his stealing steps"
3499) "           Hath clawed me in his clutch,"
3500) "         And hath shipped me intil the land,"
3501) "           As if I had never been such."

3520) "  Clown. (Sings)"

3523) "         O, a Pit of clay for to be made  "
3524) "           For such a guest is meet."
3525) "                                      Throws up [another skull]."
3526) "  Ham. There's another. Why may not that be the skull of a lawyer?"
3527) "    Where be his quiddits now, his quillets, his cases, his tenures,"
3528) "    and his tricks? Why does he suffer this rude knave now to knock"
3529) "    him about the sconce with a dirty shovel, and will not tell him"
3530) "    of his action of battery? Hum! This fellow might be in's time a"
3531) "    great buyer of land, with his statutes, his recognizances, his"

3536) "    of a pair of indentures? The very conveyances of his lands will"
3537) "    scarcely lie in this box; and must th' inheritor himself have no"

3540) "  Ham. Is not parchment made of sheepskins?"

3542) "  Ham. They are sheep and calves which seek out assurance in that. I  "
3543) "    will speak to this fellow. Whose grave's this, sirrah?"

3545) "    [Sings] O, a pit of clay for to be made"

3555) "  Ham. What woman then?"

3557) "  Ham. Who is to be buried in't?"

3559) "  Ham. How absolute the knave is! We must speak by the card, or"
3560) "    equivocation will undo us. By the Lord, Horatio, this three years"

3562) "    of the peasant comes so near the heel of the courtier he galls"
3563) "    his kibe.- How long hast thou been a grave-maker?"

3566) "  Ham. How long is that since?"
3567) "  Clown. Cannot you tell that? Every fool can tell that. It was the"
3568) "    very day that young Hamlet was born- he that is mad, and sent"

3570) "  Ham. Ay, marry, why was be sent into England?"

3572) "    or, if 'a do not, 'tis no great matter there."
3573) "  Ham. Why?"

3732) "    This grave shall have a living monument."

3734) "    Till then in patience our proceeding be."
3735) "                                                         Exeunt."
3736) "Scene II."
3737) "Elsinore. A hall in the Castle."
3738) "Enter Hamlet and Horatio."
3739) "  Ham. So much for this, sir; now shall you see the other."
3740) "    You do remember all the circumstance?"
3741) "  Hor. Remember it, my lord!"
3742) "  Ham. Sir, in my heart there was a kind of fighting"
3743) "    That would not let me sleep. Methought I lay"
3744) "    Worse than the mutinies in the bilboes. Rashly-"
3745) "    And prais'd be rashness for it; let us know,"
3746) "    Our indiscretion sometime serves us well"
3747) "    When our deep plots do pall; and that should learn us"
3748) "    There's a divinity that shapes our ends,"
3749) "    Rough-hew them how we will-"
3750) "  Hor. That is most certain."
3751) "  Ham. Up from my cabin,"
3752) "    My sea-gown scarf'd about me, in the dark"
3753) "    Grop'd I to find out them; had my desire,"
3754) "    Finger'd their packet, and in fine withdrew"
3755) "    To mine own room again; making so bold  "
3756) "    (My fears forgetting manners) to unseal"
3757) "    Their grand commission; where I found, Horatio"
3758) "    (O royal knavery!), an exact command,"
3759) "    Larded with many several sorts of reasons,"
3760) "    Importing Denmark's health, and England's too,"
3761) "    With, hoo! such bugs and goblins in my life-"
3762) "    That, on the supervise, no leisure bated,"
3763) "    No, not to stay the finding of the axe,"
3764) "    My head should be struck off."
3765) "  Hor. Is't possible?"
3766) "  Ham. Here's the commission; read it at more leisure."
3767) "    But wilt thou bear me how I did proceed?"
3768) "  Hor. I beseech you."
3769) "  Ham. Being thus benetted round with villanies,"
3770) "    Or I could make a prologue to my brains,"
3771) "    They had begun the play. I sat me down;"
3772) "    Devis'd a new commission; wrote it fair."
3773) "    I once did hold it, as our statists do,"
3774) "    A baseness to write fair, and labour'd much"
3775) "    How to forget that learning; but, sir, now  "
3776) "    It did me yeoman's service. Wilt thou know"
3777) "    Th' effect of what I wrote?"
3778) "  Hor. Ay, good my lord."
3779) "  Ham. An earnest conjuration from the King,"
3780) "    As England was his faithful tributary,"
3781) "    As love between them like the palm might flourish,"
3782) "    As peace should still her wheaten garland wear"
3783) "    And stand a comma 'tween their amities,"
3784) "    And many such-like as's of great charge,"
3785) "    That, on the view and knowing of these contents,"
3786) "    Without debatement further, more or less,"
3787) "    He should the bearers put to sudden death,"
3788) "    Not shriving time allow'd."
3789) "  Hor. How was this seal'd?"
3790) "  Ham. Why, even in that was heaven ordinant."
3791) "    I had my father's signet in my purse,"
3792) "    which was the model of that Danish seal;"
3793) "    Folded the writ up in the form of th' other,"
3794) "    Subscrib'd it, gave't th' impression, plac'd it safely,"
3795) "    The changeling never known. Now, the next day  "
3796) "    Was our sea-fight; and what to this was sequent"
3797) "    Thou know'st already."
3798) "  Hor. So Guildenstern and Rosencrantz go to't."
3799) "  Ham. Why, man, they did make love to this employment!"
3800) "    They are not near my conscience; their defeat"
3801) "    Does by their own insinuation grow."
3802) "    'Tis dangerous when the baser nature comes"
3803) "    Between the pass and fell incensed points"
3804) "    Of mighty opposites."
3805) "  Hor. Why, what a king is this!"
3806) "  Ham. Does it not, thinks't thee, stand me now upon-"
3807) "    He that hath kill'd my king, and whor'd my mother;"
3808) "    Popp'd in between th' election and my hopes;"
3809) "    Thrown out his angle for my Proper life,"
3810) "    And with such coz'nage- is't not perfect conscience"
3811) "    To quit him with this arm? And is't not to be damn'd"
3812) "    To let this canker of our nature come"
3813) "    In further evil?"
3814) "  Hor. It must be shortly known to him from England"
3815) "    What is the issue of the business there.  "
3816) "  Ham. It will be short; the interim is mine,"
3817) "    And a man's life is no more than to say 'one.'"
3818) "    But I am very sorry, good Horatio,"
3819) "    That to Laertes I forgot myself,"
3820) "    For by the image of my cause I see"
3821) "    The portraiture of his. I'll court his favours."
3822) "    But sure the bravery of his grief did put me"
3823) "    Into a tow'ring passion."
